Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa
beginner
6 min
Fatwa in Dhawi al-Arham: The Rule of No Transmitted Opinion
Rasm al-mufti rule for fatwa in dhawi al-arham without transmitted narration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rasm al-mufti rule that when no Hanafi riwaya exists on a dhawi al-arham matter, the fatwa follows the opposing school's view.

Core Idea
No transmitted opinion? Follow the opponent's view in dhawi al-arham.

The Hanafi madhhab has a defined hierarchy for issuing fatwas. When a matter relates to distant relatives (dhawi al-arham) and no transmission from the Hanafi imams exists, the mufti does not remain silent or resort to his own ijtihad—instead, he adopts the opinion of the opposing school. This concise rule, illustrated by examples from tahara, exemplifies how the madhhab pragmatically fills gaps in its transmitted corpus.

Guided Path
1
State the rule
Read the Arabic: 'الفتوى في مسائل ذوى الأرحام ما لم يكن عنه رواية كقول المخالف'. Understand: when no riwaya, fatwa is like the opponent's saying.
2
Identify its scope
The rule applies only to matters of dhawi al-arham (distant kindred inheritance). It does not apply to other areas unless analogized.
The text gives two tahara examples, showing the rule extends to certain non-inheritance cases.
3
Memorize the examples
The examples are: (1) purity of used water (طهارة الماء المستعمل), and (2) tayammum when only date-nabidh is available for wudu. In these, the Hanafi position may lack a clear riwaya, so the opponent's view is taken.
These are specific; memorize them as anchors.
4
Apply the rule in fatwa practice
When a question on dhawi al-arham arises, first check if there is a riwaya. If none, adopt the view of the opposing school (e.g., Shafi'i).
This is a rasm al-mufti instruction, not a substantive ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفتوى في مسائل ذوى الأرحام ما لم يكن عنه رواية كقول المخالف، كما في طهارة الماء المستعمل والتيمم فقط عند عدم غير نبيذ التمر.
The fatwa in matters of dhawi al-arham, when there is no narration about it, is like the saying of the opponent, as in the purity of used water and tayammum only when there is no [alternative] other than date-nabidh.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage presents a rasm rule for the mufti: in cases of inheritance for distant relatives (dhawi al-arham) where no explicit transmitted opinion (riwaya) exists from the Hanafi imams, the fatwa is given according to the opposing school's view (kawl al-mukhalif). The examples given are from tahara: the ruling on used water and tayammum when only date-nabidh is available, where the Hanafi position might not have a clear narration, so the opponent's view is adopted.

Key Terms
ذوي الأرحام (dhawi al-arham): Relatives by uterine or cognate ties, not covered by the primary Quranic heirs or agnates; a category of distant kindred in Hanafi inheritance law.
لا رواية (la riwaya): No transmitted opinion from the Hanafi imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) on the specific issue.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what should a Hanafi mufti do when there is no transmitted opinion (riwaya) in a matter of dhawi al-arham?
A) Issue a fatwa based on his own ijtihad.
B) Follow the opinion of the opposing school.
C) Refrain from giving a fatwa until a narration is found.
D) Use qiyas to derive a ruling.

Key Takeaway
When no Hanafi transmitted opinion exists on a dhawi al-arham matter, the mufti adopts the opposing school's view.

Ruling Preferring Abu Yusuf in Judicial Matters
Rule preferring Abu Yusuf's opinion in judicial matters

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rasm al-mufti rule regarding Abu Yusuf's opinion in judicial matters and apply it to a new scenario.

Core Idea
In judicial matters, the Hanafi fatwa follows Abu Yusuf due to his practical experience.

This passage establishes a specific rasm al-mufti rule: when issuing fatwas on judicial matters (qada), the mufti should rely on the opinion of Abu Yusuf rather than Abu Hanifa or Muhammad. The reason is Abu Yusuf's greater practical experience as a judge, which gave him specialized knowledge. This rule is supported by authoritative Hanafi texts and even by Abu Hanifa's own deference to Abu Yusuf on a related issue.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule
State the rule: fatwa in qada matters is on the opinion of Abu Yusuf.
2
Identify the reason
Note the rationale: ziyadat tajribah (increased practical experience).
Abu Yusuf served as chief judge under Harun al-Rashid.
3
Identify the sources
List the Hanafi texts cited: al-Qunyah, al-Bazzaziyyah, al-Bahr, Sharh al-Bayri.
These are authoritative later Hanafi references.
4
Apply the rule
Use the example of the silent defendant: Abu Yusuf's view is to imprison until response, and fatwa follows that.
This illustrates the practical impact.
5
Note the scope
Recognize that this rule applies to judiciary and closely related fields (e.g., testimonies), not all fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفتوى في القضاء وما يتعلق بها ] الثانية: ما في "البحر" قبيل فصل الحبس، قال: وفي "القنية" من باب المفتي: الفتوى على قول أبي يوسف فيما يتعلق بالقضاء؛ لزيادة تجربته. وكذا في "البزازية" من "القضاء" أي لحصول زيادة العلم له بتجربته، ولهذا أبو حنيفة عن القول بأن الصدقة أفضل من حج التطوع، لما حج وعرف مشقته. زاد في "شرح البيري على الأشباه": أن الفتوى على قول أبي يوسف أيضا في الشهادات قلت لكن هي من توابع القضاء. وفي "البحر" من كتاب الدعوى: "لو" سكت المدعى عليه ولم يجب، ينزل منكرا عندهما، أما عند أبي يوسف فيحبس إلى أن يجيب كما قال الإمام السرخسي والفتوى على قول أبي يوسف فيما يتعلق بالقضاء، كما في "القنية" و"البزازية"، فلذا أفتيت بأنه يحبس إلى أن يجيب.
Fatwa on judicial matters and what pertains to them. Second: What is in al-Bahr before the chapter on imprisonment: he said: In al-Qunyah from the chapter on the mufti: The fatwa is on the saying of Abu Yusuf in matters related to judiciary due to his increased experience. And likewise in al-Bazzaziyyah from the chapter on judiciary: meaning because he gained additional knowledge through his experience. For this reason, Abu Hanifa [referred to him] regarding the statement that charity is better than supererogatory Hajj, because he performed Hajj and knew its difficulty. In Sharh al-Bayri on al-Ashbah, it is added: The fatwa is on the saying of Abu Yusuf also in testimonies, but I say they are subsidiary to judiciary. And in al-Bahr from the book of claims: 'If the defendant remains silent and does not answer, he is considered a denier according to them both. However, according to Abu Yusuf, he is imprisoned until he answers, as Imam al-Sarakhsi said, and the fatwa is on the saying of Abu Yusuf in matters related to judiciary, as in al-Qunyah and al-Bazzaziyyah. Therefore, I gave a fatwa that he is imprisoned until he answers.'
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage establishes a rasm rule: in matters of judiciary (qada) and its related fields (such as testimonies), the relied-upon position for fatwa is that of Abu Yusuf, not Abu Hanifa or Muhammad. The reason given is Abu Yusuf's extensive practical experience in judicial matters, which gave him additional knowledge. This is supported by quoting from multiple authoritative sources (al-Bahr, al-Qunyah, al-Bazzaziyyah). An example is provided: in a case where the defendant remains silent, the Hanafi school generally treats him as denying, but Abu Yusuf's opinion is to imprison him until he responds, and that is the fatwa. The passage also notes that even Abu Hanifa deferred to Abu Yusuf in a matter concerning the virtue of charity over Hajj after Abu Yusuf experienced Hajj.

Key Terms
القضاء (al-qada): Judicial matters, including court proceedings, rulings, and related procedures.
تجربة (tajribah): Practical experience, specifically in judicial matters, which gives additional knowledge.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is Abu Yusuf's opinion preferred in judicial matters?
A) He was the most knowledgeable Hanafi imam overall.
B) He had greater practical experience in judiciary.
C) Abu Hanifa explicitly ordered his followers to follow him.
D) His opinions are easier to implement.

Key Takeaway
In judicial matters, the mufta bihi is Abu Yusuf's opinion due to his practical experience.

Fatwa Preference in Dhawi al-Arham: Following Imam Muhammad
Rule preferring Muhammad's opinion in dhawi al-arham inheritance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the operative opinion for fatwa in dhawi al-arham cases and explain why Muhammad al-Shaybani's opinion is preferred.

Core Idea
In all inheritance matters of dhawi al-arham, the fatwa is on the opinion of Muhammad al-Shaybani.

This lesson introduces a specific rule from Rasm al-Mufti: when issuing fatwas on inheritance distribution among distant relatives (dhawi al-arham), the mufti must rely on the opinion of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani. This rule is based on the fact that his opinion is the more famous of two narrations from Imam Abu Hanifa himself. The rule is explicitly stated in authoritative Hanafi works like al-Multaqa, Sakb al-Anhur, and al-Kafi.

Guided Path
1
Identify the domain
Recognize that the rule applies specifically to dhawi al-arham (distant relatives) inheritance, not to all heirs.
2
State the operative rule
The rule, as quoted from al-Multaqa, is: 'Bi qawl Muhammad yufta' — by the saying of Muhammad the fatwa is given.
3
Understand the basis
This opinion is the more famous (ashhar) of two narrations from Abu Hanifa, meaning it is the stronger and more widely accepted transmission.
The other narration is from a different student or chain.
4
Recognize the sources
Note that this rule is confirmed by multiple later authorities: Sakb al-Anhur, Sharh Fara'id by Siraj al-Din, and al-Kafi.
5
Apply the rule in fatwa
When a question on dhawi al-arham arises, the mufti must issue the fatwa based on Muhammad's calculation and method.
This overrides any other preference or personal opinion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفتوى في مسائل ذوي الأرحام] الثالثة: ما في متن "الملتقى" وغيره في مسألة القسمة على ذوي الأرحام: "وبقول محمد يفتى". قال في "سكب الأنهر". أي في جميع توريث ذوي الأرحام، وهو أشهر الروايتين عن الإمام أبي حنيفة وبه يفتى كذا قاله الشيخ سراج الدين في "شرح فرائضه". وقال في "الكافي": "وقول" محمد أشهر الروايتين عن أبي حنيفة في جميع ذوي الأرحام، وعليه الفتوى.
Fatwa on matters of dhawi al-arham. Third: What is in the text of al-Multaqa and others on the issue of division among dhawi al-arham: 'And by the saying of Muhammad the fatwa is given.' He said in Sakb al-Anhur: i.e., in all inheritance of dhawi al-arham, and it is the more famous of the two narrations from Imam Abu Hanifa, and with it fatwa is given. Thus said Shaykh Siraj al-Din in his Sharh Fara'id. And he said in al-Kafi: 'The saying of Muhammad is the more famous of the two narrations from Abu Hanifa in all dhawi al-arham, and upon it is the fatwa.'
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage presents a second rasm rule concerning dhawi al-arham: the fatwa in all matters of inheritance for distant relatives (dhawi al-arham) is on the opinion of Muhammad al-Shaybani. This is based on multiple sources: al-Multaqa, Sakb al-Anhur, and al-Kafi. It also notes that this opinion is the more famous of two narrations from Abu Hanifa (i.e., the stronger transmission from him). Thus, in contrast to the earlier rule about following the opponent when no narration exists, here a clear Hanafi opinion exists and is preferred.

Key Terms
محمد (Muhammad): Muhammad al-Shaybani (d. 189 AH), the foremost student of Abu Hanifa, author of the six core books of Zahir al-Riwaya.
أشهر الروايتين (ashhar al-riwayatayn): The more famous of two narrations (from Abu Hanifa), indicating the stronger or more widely accepted transmission.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which Hanafi imam's opinion is the basis for fatwa in all inheritance of dhawi al-arham?
A) Abu Hanifa's opinion as the more famous narration.
B) Abu Yusuf's opinion due to his judicial experience.
C) Muhammad al-Shaybani's opinion.
D) The opinion of the opposing school.

Key Takeaway
For dhawi al-arham inheritance, the mufta bihi is the opinion of Muhammad al-Shaybani, being the more famous narration from Abu Hanifa.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa
beginner

Preferring Zahir al-Riwaya over Shadhdh Narrations
Rule that zahir al-riwaya takes precedence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that zahir al-riwaya takes precedence over shadhdh narrations when determining the mufta bihi position in Hanafi fiqh.

Core Idea
Any opinion deviating from zahir al-riwaya is retracted and no longer a valid mujtahid saying.

In the Hanafi school, not all transmitted opinions carry equal weight. The well-known corpus known as zahir al-riwaya forms the authoritative foundation for fatwa and qada. This lesson presents a fundamental rule from the Rasm al-Mufti literature: any narration that falls outside this corpus is considered retracted (marju' 'anhu) and cannot be used unless specific authorization is given. Understanding this hierarchy is essential for anyone issuing fatwa or judging according to the Hanafi madhhab.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the maxim
Commit to memory the Arabic maxim: 'ما خرج عن ظاهر الرواية فهو مرجوع عنه'. This is the core rule of this lesson.
The maxim is from al-Bahr and appears in the passage.
2
Understand the consequence
If an opinion is 'marju' 'anhu', it means the mujtahid (Imam) has retracted it; it is no longer considered a valid saying of the madhhab.
The passage explicitly says it 'does not remain a saying of the mujtahid'.
3
Apply to the muqallid judge
A judge who is a muqallid (follower) may only judge by the apparent (zahir) of the madhhab, not by shadhdh narrations, unless the scholars explicitly state that fatwa is upon that narration.
This is a practical restriction on judicial authority.
4
Apply when zahir is silent
If an issue is not found in zahir al-riwaya but is established in another riwaya, then it is necessary to resort to that other riwaya.
This shows that the rule is not absolute; non-zahir narrations become binding when no zahir exists.
5
Identify the istithna
The only exception is when the scholars explicitly state that fatwa is upon a shadhdh narration. In that case, it overrides the general rule.
This istithna is based on specific textual directives.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامسة ما في قضاء "البحر": من أن ما خرج عن ظاهر الرواية، فهو مرجوع عنه. عنه. والمرجوع والمرجوع عنه لم يبق قولا للمجتهد كما ذكروه. وقدمنا "أنفع المسائل": أن القاضي المقلد لا يجوز له أن يحكم إلا بما هو ظاهر المذهب لا بالرواية الشاذة إلا أن ينصّوا على أن الفتوى عليها. وفي قضاء الفوائت من "البحر": "أن المسألة إذا لم تذكر في ظاهر عن الرواية وثبتت في رواية أخرى تعين المصير إليها".
Fifth: What is in the judiciary section of al-Bahr: that what departs from zahir al-riwaya is retracted (marju' 'anhu). And the retracted and the retracted from it do not remain a saying of the mujtahid, as they have mentioned. And we have previously presented in Anfa' al-Masa'il: that a judge who is a muqallid is not permitted to judge except by what is the apparent (zahir) of the madhhab, not by a shadhdh narration, unless they explicitly state that the fatwa is upon it. And in the section on Qada al-Fawa'it from al-Bahr: 'that if an issue is not mentioned in the apparent (zahir) of the riwaya and is established in another narration, then it is necessary to resort to it.'
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage presents a rasm rule regarding the use of narrations: the well-known transmitted opinions (zahir al-riwaya) are the authoritative positions of the madhhab. Any opinion that deviates from zahir al-riwaya is considered 'marju' 'anhu' (retracted) and thus no longer a valid saying of the mujtahid. For a muqallid judge, only the apparent (zahir) of the madhhab may be used in judgments, not shadhdh (irregular) narrations, except if the scholars explicitly say fatwa is upon that narration. However, if an issue is not mentioned in zahir al-riwaya but is found in another riwaya (even if not zahir), then that other riwaya must be followed.

Key Terms
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwaya): The well-known, widely transmitted opinions from the Hanafi imams (especially from the six books of Muhammad al-Shaybani).
رواية شاذة (riwaya shadhdhah): An irregular or less common narration that is not part of the well-known transmitted corpus.
مرجوع عنه (marju' 'anhu): Retracted; an opinion that has been withdrawn and is no longer considered a valid saying of the mujtahid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling for a muqallid judge regarding a shadhdh narration according to this passage?
A) He may use it if it is more convenient.
B) He cannot use it unless the scholars explicitly state that fatwa is upon it.
C) He must prefer it over zahir al-riwaya.
D) He should seek a new ijtihad.

Key Takeaway
The general rule is that only zahir al-riwaya opinions are authoritative for fatwa; exceptions require explicit authorization.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa
intermediate
6 min
Following the Evidence (Muqtada al-Dalil) in Fatwa
Rule that the mufti should not deviate from evidence-based ruling

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principle of muqtada al-dalil to determine the mufta bihi ruling when evidence conflicts with weak narrations.

Core Idea
When evidence clearly indicates a ruling, the mufti must follow it, even if a weak narration suggests otherwise.

In the Hanafi school, a mufti is not merely a transmitter of narrations; he is a scholar of legal reasoning. This lesson introduces a pivotal rule from Sharh al-Munyah: the mufti must adhere to what the evidence (dalil) requires, known as muqtada al-dalil. The passage discusses the pillars of prayer—specifically tuma'ninah (calmness), qawmah (standing after bowing), and jalsah (sitting between prostrations)—where narrations from Imam Abu Hanifa differ on whether they are sunnah or wajib. Despite these narrations, the evidence points to obligation, and the mufti should not deviate from that evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested ruling
The passage mentions tuma'ninah, qawmah, and jalsah in prayer. The dispute: are they sunnah or wajib? Recognize that there are narrations from Abu Hanifa suggesting sunnah.
This is a classic intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf where the imam's own statements appear contradictory.
2
Understand the principle of muqtada al-dalil
The phrase 'mqtada al-dalil al-wujub' means 'the requirement of the evidence is obligation.' This is a methodological rule: when evidence (e.g., the Prophet's command 'pray as you see me pray') points to a ruling, that ruling should be adopted over isolated narrations.
This is a form of tarjih (preferencing) based on diraya (knowledge) over riwaya (narration).
3
Apply the rule to the case
Even though there are narrations from Abu Hanifa indicating sunnah, the evidence from the Sunnah and general principles indicates wujub. Therefore, the mufta bihi (operative ruling) is wujub.
The mufti must weigh the strength of evidence; diraya can clarify the intended ruling.
4
Extract the fatwa hierarchy lesson
This rule fits within the fatwa hierarchy: Zahir al-Riwayah might have different narrations, but the later scholars (mutakhkhirin) used diraya to settle the issue. The mufta bihi is determined by evidence, not by counting narrations.
This is a foundational rule in Rasm al-Mufti.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادسة: ما في "شرح المنية" في بحث تعديل الأركان بعد ما ذكر اختلاف الرواية عن الإمام في الطمأنينة هل هي سنة أو واجبة؟ وكذا القومة والجلسة، قال: وأنت" علمت أن مقتضى الدليل الوجوب كما
Sixth: What is in Sharh al-Munyah in the discussion of the correct performance of the pillars after he mentioned the difference in narration from the Imam concerning calmness (tuma'ninah) – is it sunnah or wajib? And likewise standing (qawmah) and sitting (jalsah). He said: 'And you know that the requirement of the evidence is obligation, as...'
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage is incomplete. It introduces a rule about not deviating from the knowledge (diraya) derived from evidence. The context is the discussion of the essential acts of prayer: whether the calmness (tuma'ninah) and the standing and sitting after bowing are sunnah or wajib. The text states that the evidence (dalil) indicates obligation (wujub). This suggests that despite some narrations suggesting otherwise, the mufti should not deviate from what the evidence requires. The passage cuts off before completing the point, but the principle is clear: when evidence points to a ruling, the mufti must follow it.

Key Terms
الدراية (al-diraya): Knowledge derived from evidence and reasoning, as opposed to mere narration (riwaya).
مقتضى الدليل (muqtada al-dalil): What the evidence requires; the logical conclusion from the proof.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is sunnah.
B) It is wajib.
C) It is optional.
D) The text does not specify.

Key Takeaway
The mufti must follow what the evidence requires, even if a weak narration says otherwise.

Preferring Istihsan over Qiyas with Exceptions
Rasm rule listing exceptions to the general preference of istihsan over qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the general rule preferring istihsan over qiyas, identify the exceptions as enumerated in classical texts, and explain the Hanafi disagreement on the meaning of 'tarjih'.

Core Idea
Istihsan generally overrides qiyas, but exceptions exist—and scholars disagree on both the number of exceptions and whether acting by the preponderated qiyas is permissible.

In this passage, we learn a key rule of Hanafi fatwa: when a case involves both istihsan and qiyas, we usually follow istihsan. However, the rule is not absolute. There are exceptions, and even the meaning of 'preference' (tarjih) is debated among Hanafi masters. Understanding this hierarchy and its disagreements is essential for any mufti tasked with issuing sound fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
Recognize the blanket statement: when qiyas and istihsan both apply to a case, istihsan is preferred (yutarjahu al-istihsan). This is the default position.
The rule is found in 'the general books' (عامة الكتب), indicating wide acceptance.
2
Note the existence of exceptions
The rule is not absolute; there are exceptional cases where qiyas takes precedence. The source mentions two counts: 11 in 'Ajnās al-Nāṭifī' and 22 according to Najm al-Dīn al-Nasafī.
The exact number is disputed; later scholars may have expanded the list.
3
Understand the debate on 'tarjih'
Two interpretations exist: (1) According to 'al-Talwīḥ', tarjih means acting by the preponderant (istihsan) is determined and the preponderated (qiyas) must be abandoned. (2) According to Fakhr al-Islam, tarjih merely indicates priority, so acting by the preponderated is still permissible.
This distinction affects the mufti's obligation to avoid giving fatwa by qiyas in general cases.
4
Apply to fatwa practice
The majority relied-upon view (mufta bihi) is that the preponderated evidence cannot be acted upon. Therefore, a mufti must use istihsan in all cases except the enumerated exceptions, where qiyas governs.
The exceptions are fixed; a mufti should memorize them or know where to find them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابعة ما في عامة الكتب: من أنه إذا كان في مسألة قياس واستحسان ترجح الاستحسان على القياس إلا في مسائل، وهي إحدى عشرة مسألة على ما في "أجناس الناطفي"، وذكرها العلامة ابن نجيم في شرحه على "المنار"، ثم ذكر أن نجم الدين النسفي أوصلها إلى اثنتين وعشرين. وذكر قبله عن "التلويح": أن الصحيح أن معنى الرجحان هنا تعين العمل بالراجح، وترك العمل بالمرجوح. وظاهر كلام فخر الإسلام أنه الأولوية، حتى يجوز العمل بالمرجوح.
The fourth: That which is in the general books: that if in a matter there is qiyas and istihsan, istihsan is preferred over qiyas except in certain matters, and they are eleven matters according to what is in "Ajnās al-Nāṭifī", and the scholar Ibn Nujaym mentioned them in his commentary on "al-Manār", then he mentioned that Najm al-Dīn al-Nasafī increased them to twenty-two. And before him it was mentioned from "al-Talwīḥ": that the correct meaning of 'preference' here is the determination to act by the preponderant and to abandon acting by the preponderated. And the apparent meaning of the words of Fakhr al-Islam is that it is preferential, such that acting by the preponderated is permissible.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage presents a rasm rule concerning the hierarchy of legal reasoning within the Hanafi school: when a matter has both a qiyas (analogical reasoning) and an istihsan (juristic preference), the istihsan is generally given precedence over qiyas. However, there are exceptions. The author notes that in "Ajnās al-Nāṭifī" the exceptions are enumerated as eleven, while Najm al-Dīn al-Nasafī expanded them to twenty-two. This indicates a difference of opinion among later Hanafi scholars regarding the exact count. The passage also clarifies the meaning of 'tarjīḥ' (preference): according to "al-Talwīḥ", it means that acting by the preponderant (istihsan) is determined and acting by the preponderated (qiyas) must be abandoned. In contrast, Fakhr al-Islam (al-Bazdawi) understood it as a mere precedence of one over the other, meaning that acting by the preponderated (qiyas) is still permissible, though not preferred. This distinction affects the degree of obligation in following the preferred opinion. The rule is fundamental for a mufti: in most cases, one should give fatwa based on istihsan, but must memorize the exceptional cases where qiyas overrides.

Key Terms
استحسان (istiḥsān): Juristic preference; deviating from strict analogy for a stronger reason based on a source or need.
قياس (qiyās): Analogical deduction; extending a ruling from an original case to a new case based on common effective cause.
رجحان (tarjīḥ): Preference given to one opinion or evidence over another.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, how many exceptions are there to the rule of preferring istihsan over qiyas according to 'Ajnās al-Nāṭifī'?
A) 11
B) 22
C) 5
D) 15

Key Takeaway
Istihsan is generally preponderant over qiyas, but the mufti must know the enumerated exceptions and understand that the majority view obligates abandoning the preponderated evidence.

Zahir al-Riwayah vs Shadh Riwayah: Preference Rule for Mufti and Qadi
Rasm rule on preferring the well-known transmission over rare ones

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Zahir al-Riwayah as the authoritative corpus, state the rule that muqallids must prefer it over shadh riwayah, and apply the exception when Zahir al-Riwayah is silent.

Core Idea
Zahir al-Riwayah is authoritative; shadh riwayah is followed only if explicitly allowed or when Zahir is silent.

In the Hanafi madhhab, not all transmissions carry equal weight. The well-known corpus called Zahir al-Riwayah is the primary source for fatwa and judiciary. This lesson clarifies the hierarchy, explaining when a muqallid must reject a rare transmission and when an exception applies.

Guided Path
1
Define Zahir al-Riwayah
Understand that Zahir al-Riwayah refers to the six core books of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani: al-Mabsut, al-Jami' al-Saghir, al-Jami' al-Kabir, al-Siyar al-Saghir, al-Siyar al-Kabir, and al-Ziyadat. These are the most widely transmitted and authoritative texts.
Memorize the list of six books.
2
Understand the retraction rule
Any opinion that departs from Zahir al-Riwayah is considered 'marju' anhu' (retracted). Such opinions are no longer valid statements of the mujtahid.
This elevates Zahir al-Riwayah above all other transmissions.
3
Apply the rule to a qadi
A qadi (muqallid) is not permitted to judge by a shadh (rare) riwayah, unless the jurists explicitly state that the fatwa is upon it.
The burden of proof is on the shadh transmission.
4
Identify the exception
If a matter is not mentioned in Zahir al-Riwayah at all, but is established in another riwayah, then that riwayah must be followed. This is an exception to the general rule.
The exception comes from the chapter of missed prayers (qada' al-fawa'it).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامسة ما في قضاء "البحر": من أن ما خرج عن ظاهر الرواية، فهو مرجوع عنه. والمرجوع عنه لم يبق قولا للمجتهد كما ذكروه. وقدمنا "أنفع المسائل": أن القاضي المقلد لا يجوز له أن يحكم إلا بما هو ظاهر المذهب لا بالرواية الشاذة إلا أن ينصّوا على أن الفتوى عليها. وفي قضاء الفوائت من "البحر": "أن المسألة إذا لم تذكر في ظاهر الرواية وثبتت في رواية أخرى تعين المصير إليها".
The fifth: That which is in the judiciary section of "al-Bahr": that whatever departs from Zahir al-Riwayah is a retracted opinion. And the retracted opinion is no longer a statement of the mujtahid as they mentioned. And we previously presented "Anfa' al-Masail": that a qadi who is a muqallid is not permitted to judge except by what is the apparent (zahir) of the madhhab, not by the shadh riwayah, unless they (the jurists) explicitly state that the fatwa is upon it. And in the judiciary of missed prayers from "al-Bahr": "If a matter is not mentioned in Zahir al-Riwayah, but is established in another riwayah, then it must be followed."
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage establishes a crucial rasm rule regarding the weight of transmissions within the Hanafi school. "Zahir al-Riwayah" refers to the six core books of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani (al-Mabsut, al-Jami' al-Saghir, etc.). Any opinion that deviates from these is considered "marjū' 'anhu" (retracted) and thus not a valid position of the school. The rule explicitly states that a qadi (muqallid) cannot judge based on a shadh (rare) transmission unless the scholars have explicitly said that the fatwa is based on it. However, the passage also notes an exception from "al-Bahr" in the chapter of missed prayers: if a matter is not mentioned in Zahir al-Riwayah but is found in another riwayah, then that riwayah must be followed. This exception shows that when the core texts are silent, recourse to other transmissions is permitted. The mufti must know which riwayah is Zahir al-Riwayah and which is shadh, and prioritize accordingly.

Key Terms
ظاهر الرواية (Ẓāhir al-Riwāyah): The well-known, mass-transmitted corpus of Hanafi law (the six books of Imam Muhammad).
رواية شاذة (riwāyah shādhah): A rare or isolated transmission not part of Zahir al-Riwayah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He must follow the other riwayah
B) He must ignore it
C) He may choose either
D) He must refer to a mujtahid

Key Takeaway
The mufti must always prioritize Zahir al-Riwayah, but when it is silent, he must follow any other established riwayah.

Not Turning Away from Diraya When Supported by Riwaya
Rasm rule on preferring diraya when supported by riwaya

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that when a diraya (proof) is supported by a riwaya, the mufti should prefer that riwaya over other conflicting riwayat in issuing fatwa.

Core Idea
Do not abandon a clear proof for a weak narration—when diraya and riwaya agree, that is the strongest opinion.

This lesson examines a subtle but critical rule for muftis: when conflicting narrations from Imam Abu Hanifa exist, the one supported by strongest proof (diraya) should be followed, even if it is not the most famous. The rule is illustrated through the example of whether tuma'ninah (poise in prayer) is obligatory or sunnah. Shaykh Kamal al-Din argues that the proof indicates obligation, and since a narration supports that, one should not turn away from that proof. This refines the earlier hierarchy: Zahir al-Riwayah is primary, but proof strength can override mere frequency.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conflicting reports
In the passage, the issue is whether tuma'ninah (and other prayer actions) is sunnah or wajib. There are narrations from Abu Hanifa on both sides.
The conflict is not between schools but within the Hanafi transmission.
2
Locate the diraya (proof)
Determine what the stronger proof (dalil) indicates. The text quotes Shaykh Kamal al-Din: 'the requirement of the proof is obligation'.
Diraya here is synonymous with dalil – rational or textual evidence.
3
Check if a riwaya supports that diraya
Identify which narration aligns with the proof. If a riwaya supports the diraya, that combination should be preferred.
The diraya points to obligation, and presumably there is a riwaya also stating obligation.
4
Apply the rule
Prefer the riwaya that agrees with the diraya. The text from al-Hawi al-Qudsi reinforces: 'when narrations differ, take the strongest in proof'.
This overrides the mere frequency of transmission; proof strength becomes decisive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادسة: ما في "شرح المنية" في بحث تعديل الأركان بعد ما ذكر اختلاف الرواية عن الإمام في الطمأنينة هل هي سنة أو واجبة؟ وكذا القومة والجلسة، قال: "وأنت علمت أن مقتضى الدليل الوجوب كما قاله الشيخ كمال الدين، ولا ينبغي أن يعدل عن الدراية إذا وافقتها رواية". والدراية – بالدال المهملة - تستعمل بمعنى الدليل كما في "المستصفى". ويؤيده ما في آخر "الحاوي القدسي": "إذا اختلفت الروايات عن أبي حنيفة في مسألة، فالأولى بالأخذ أقواها حجة".
The sixth: That which is in "Sharh al-Munya" in the discussion of the modification of the pillars (of prayer), after he mentioned the difference in narration from the Imam concerning tranquility (tuma'ninah) – whether it is sunnah or obligatory? And likewise the standing and the sitting. He said: "And you knew that the requirement of the proof is obligation, as Shaykh Kamal al-Din said, and it is not appropriate to turn away from diraya (knowledge/reasoning) when a riwaya (narration) supports it." And diraya – with the unpointed dal – is used in the meaning of proof (dalil) as in "al-Mustasfa". And it is supported by what is at the end of "al-Hawi al-Qudsi": "When narrations from Abu Hanifah differ concerning a matter, the most preferable to take is the strongest in proof."
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage deals with a specific rasm rule concerning the interplay between diraya (rational reasoning or proof) and riwaya (transmitted reports). The context is the discussion on whether the actions in prayer (tuma'ninah, standing, sitting) are sunnah or obligatory. The text quotes Shaykh Kamal al-Din saying that the proof indicates obligation (i.e., the diraya points to obligation), and then states that one should not turn away from this diraya if it is supported by a riwaya. The term 'diraya' here is defined as 'dalīl' (proof), as in al-Ghazali's al-Mustasfa. It means that when a rational or textual proof (diraya) is in harmony with a transmitted narration (riwaya), that combination should be followed. Moreover, the passage cites al-Hawi al-Qudsi that when narrations from Abu Hanifah conflict, the one with the strongest proof (dalil) should be taken. This rule refines the earlier hierarchy: while Zahir al-Riwayah is primary, if there is a clear proof (diraya) that aligns with a particular riwaya, that riwaya may be preferred even if it is not the well-known one. The mufti must weigh both the authority of the transmission and the strength of the underlying evidence.

Key Terms
دراية (dirāyah): Knowledge based on proof and reasoning, often synonymous with dalil in this context.
الطمأنينة (ṭuma'nīnah): Tranquility/poise in prayer; the state of stillness between movements.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) A rare transmission
B) Knowledge from experience
C) Proof (dalil)
D) The opinion of the majority

Key Takeaway
When a clear proof (diraya) supports a transmission (riwaya), that riwaya must be preferred over conflicting narrations, even if less widely transmitted.

The Seventh Rule: Preferring the Riwaya That Avoids Takfir
Rasm rule that in doubt, avoid declaring a Muslim an unbeliever

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rasm rule regarding takfir and apply it to a scenario where a Muslim's words could be interpreted favorably.

Core Idea
If any riwaya (even weak) avoids takfir, adopt it – disbelief is too grave to declare without certainty.

The gravity of declaring someone an unbeliever cannot be overstated. The Hanafi school, through this rasm rule, instructs muftis to exercise extreme caution: as long as any narration or interpretation exists that avoids takfir, even if that narration is weak, the fatwa must refrain from declaring disbelief. This lesson unpacks the rationale and application of this principle.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the gravity of kufr
Understand that declaring someone a kafir has severe worldly and otherworldly consequences. This gravity justifies the cautious approach.
The text says: 'الكفر شيء عظيم' – disbelief is a tremendous matter.
2
Identify the presence of any riwaya supporting non-kufr
Check if there exists any riwaya (narration) that suggests the person’s words or actions do not constitute disbelief. This includes weak riwayat.
The passage explicitly includes 'ولو رواية ضعيفة – even if a weak riwaya'.
3
Apply the rule
If such a riwaya exists, or if the words can be given a favorable interpretation (mahmil hasan), the mufti must avoid declaring takfir. This is the operative ruling.
The rule is derived from the principle of preserving the Muslim's status unless certainty is established.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السابعة ما في "البحر" من باب المرتد نقلا عن "الفتاوى الصغرى": الكفر شيء عظيم فلا أجعل المؤمن كافرا متى وجدت رواية أنه لا يكفر. ثم قال والذي تحرر أنه لا يفتى بكفر مسلم أمكن حمل كلامه على محمل حسن، أو كان في كفره اختلاف ولو رواية ضعيفة.
The seventh: That which is in "al-Bahr" from the chapter of apostate, quoting from "al-Fatawa al-Sughra": "Disbelief is a grave matter, so I will not declare a believer an unbeliever whenever I find a riwaya that he is not an unbeliever." Then he said: "And what is established is that one should not give a fatwa of disbelief of a Muslim if it is possible to interpret his words in a good way, or if there is a difference of opinion about his disbelief, even if it is a weak riwaya."
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage presents a rasm rule concerning the extreme caution required in matters of takfir (declaring someone an apostate). The quote from al-Fatawa al-Sughra emphasizes that disbelief is a tremendous matter, so a mufti should never declare a Muslim an unbeliever as long as there exists any riwaya (even if weak) that says he is not an unbeliever. The rule is that if a person's words or actions could possibly be interpreted in a good manner (or if there is any divergence of opinion on whether those words constitute kufr), then the fatwa should avoid takfir. This reflects the Hanafi principle of preserving the status of Islam for a Muslim, and only removing it with certainty. Even a weak riwaya is sufficient to prevent a takfir fatwa. The mufti must give the benefit of the doubt and seek any excuse to avoid declaring disbelief.

Key Terms
تكفير (takfīr): The act of declaring someone an unbeliever (kafir).
محمل حسن (maḥmil ḥasan): A favorable interpretation of someone's words/actions that avoids the charge of disbelief.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) The person is still declared an unbeliever
C) The fatwa of kufr is avoided
D) A stronger riwayah must be sought

Key Takeaway
A mufti must never declare a Muslim an unbeliever if any interpretation or riwaya (even weak) supports avoiding takfir.

6 min
Obligation to Take the Retracted Opinion
Rasm rule on retraction of a mujtahid's opinion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule of retraction (ruju) in Hanafi ifta: determine when a mujtahid's earlier opinion becomes invalid and when both opinions remain potential.

Core Idea
A mujtahid's retracted opinion is like an abrogated ruling: it must be abandoned.

When a mujtahid changes his view, his earlier opinion is no longer part of his madhhab. The mufti must seek and act upon the later opinion. However, uncertainty about which opinion is later complicates this. This lesson clarifies the rule from al-Bahr and Sharh al-Tahrir, essential for navigating conflicting narrations from a single imam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule
Read the passage and extract the core rule: a retracted opinion (marju'anhu) ceases to be part of the mujtahid's madhhab and becomes like a mansukh (abrogated) ruling.
This is the first principle: retraction invalidates the earlier opinion.
2
Determine the condition for retraction
According to Sharh al-Tahrir, retraction is only effective if we know the later opinion with certainty. If we do not know which opinion is later, we simply narrate both without declaring retraction.
Certainty is key; without it, both opinions remain equally potential.
3
Apply the rule to a scenario
Given two conflicting opinions from Imam Abu Hanifa, determine if we have evidence of a later opinion. If yes, the earlier is invalid; if no, both are valid for potential fatwa.
This step tests your ability to operationalize the rule.
4
Distinguish from similar cases
Compare with cases of ikhtilaf between imams (e.g., Abu Hanifa vs Shafi'i). The rule of retraction applies only within a single mujtahid's ijtihad, not between different mujtahids.
Keep the scope clear: internal retraction, not inter-madhhab conflict.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثامنة: ما في "البحر" مما قدمناه قريبا: "من أن المرجوع عنه لم يبق مذهبا للمجتهد، وحينئذ فيجب طلب القول الذي رجع إليه والعمل به؛ لأن الأول صار بمنزلة الحكم المنسوخ". وفي "البحر" أيضا عن "التوشيح": أن ما رجع عنه المجتهد لا يجوز الأخذ به. وذكر في "شرح التحرير": "إن علم المتأخر فهو مذهبه، ويكون الأول منسوخا، وإلا حكي عنه القولان من غير أن يحكم على أحدهما بالرجوع".
The eighth: That which is in "al-Bahr" from what we recently mentioned: "That the retracted opinion is no longer a madhhab of the mujtahid, and then it is obligatory to seek the statement which he retracted to and act upon it; because the first has become like an abrogated ruling." And in "al-Bahr" also from "al-Tawshih": that what the mujtahid retracted from is not permissible to act upon. And it is mentioned in "Sharh al-Tahrir": "If the later (opinion) is known, then it is his madhhab, and the first is abrogated; otherwise, both opinions are narrated without ruling one of them as a retraction."
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage addresses the legal effect of a mujtahid's retraction (ruju') of a previously held opinion. The text from al-Bahr establishes that a retracted opinion ceases to be part of the mujtahid's madhhab; it becomes analogous to a mansukh (abrogated) ruling. Consequently, it is wajib to search for and act upon the later opinion (which the mujtahid reverted to). The same source, quoting al-Tawshih, reiterates that acting upon the retracted opinion is not permissible. The passage then qualifies this with a nuance from Sharh al-Tahrir: if the later opinion is known (i.e., we are certain the mujtahid retracted), then the first is abrogated. However, if we do not have certainty about which opinion is later, then we simply narrate both opinions without declaring one as retracted. This is crucial for a mufti: when there are multiple opinions attributed to an imam (e.g., Abu Hanifah), one must determine if there is a known retraction. If not, both opinions remain potential positions, and the mufti may need to weigh them without assuming abrogation.

Key Terms
مرجوع عنه (marjū' 'anhu): An opinion that the mujtahid has retracted and abandoned.
منسوخ (mansūkh): Abrogated; a ruling that has been replaced by a later one.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the status of an opinion that a mujtahid has retracted?
A) It remains a valid opinion but less preferred
B) It is like an abrogated ruling and cannot be acted upon
C) It can still be used if no other opinion exists
D) It is considered an error and must be avoided

Key Takeaway
A mufti must act on the mujtahid's latest known opinion; a retracted earlier opinion is like an abrogated ruling and cannot be followed.

6 min
Rule on Adopting the Retracted Opinion of a Mujtahid
Explaining the necessity of following the mujtahid's final opinion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that the mujtahid's retracted opinion is abrogated and identify the binding opinion for fatwa.

Core Idea
A retracted opinion is like an abrogated ruling — only the final opinion is the madhhab.

When a mujtahid changes his view, the earlier opinion loses its authority. This lesson explains why the mufti must seek the mujtahid's final position and treat the earlier one as invalid. Understanding this prevents issuing fatwas based on outdated rulings.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the rule
State the rule: the retracted opinion (al-marju' 'anhu) is no longer part of the mujtahid's madhhab.
This is an axiomatic principle in Hanafi fatwa methodology.
2
Identify the consequence
Understand that the mufti must seek the final opinion (al-marju' ilayh) and act upon it.
The first opinion is like an abrogated (mansukh) ruling.
3
Apply the condition
If the later opinion is known, it is the madhhab; if unknown, both opinions are simply reported without determining retraction.
This is from Sharh al-Tahrir: when in doubt, present both views.
4
Connect to fatwa practice
A mufti must research whether the mujtahid retracted an earlier view before issuing a fatwa.
Ignoring retraction leads to following a non-operative opinion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثامنة: ما في "البحر" مما قدمناه قريبا: "من أن المرجوع عنه لم يبق مذهبا للمجتهد، وحينئذ فيجب طلب القول الذي رجع إليه والعمل به؛ لأن الأول صار بمنزلة الحكم المنسوخ". وفي "البحر" أيضا عن "التوشيح": أن ما رجع عنه المجتهد لا يجوز الأخذ به. وذكر في "شرح التحرير": "إن علم المتأخر فهو مذهبه، ويكون الأول منسوخا، وإلا حكي عنه القولان من غير أن يحكم على أحدهما بالرجوع"
Eighth: What is in al-Bahr which we mentioned recently: 'That the retracted opinion is no longer a madhhab for the mujtahid, and thus it is obligatory to seek the opinion he retracted to and act upon it, because the first has become like an abrogated ruling.' And in al-Bahr also from al-Tawshih: 'That what the mujtahid retracted is not permissible to act upon.' And it is mentioned in Sharh al-Tahrir: 'If the later [opinion] is known, then it is his madhhab, and the first is abrogated; otherwise, both opinions are narrated from him without determining which is retracted.'
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth rule concerning the retraction (ruju') of a mujtahid. It quotes from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq that the opinion from which the mujtahid retracted no longer constitutes his madhhab. Consequently, it is obligatory (wajib) to seek the opinion he retracted to (al-marju' ilayh) and act upon it, because the first opinion is akin to an abrogated (mansukh) ruling. Another quote from al-Tawshih affirms that acting on a retracted opinion is impermissible. The passage also cites Sharh al-Tahrir, which distinguishes: if the later opinion is known, it is his madhhab and the earlier is abrogated; if not, both opinions are simply reported without determining which one was retracted. This rule is crucial for muftis to avoid issuing fatwas based on outdated positions of the imam.

Key Terms
المرجوع عنه (al-marju' 'anhu): The opinion that the mujtahid later retracted or abandoned.
المرجوع إليه (al-marju' ilayh): The final opinion that the mujtahid adopted after retraction.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the rule in this lesson, what is the status of an opinion a mujtahid has retracted?
A) It remains a valid alternative for fatwa.
B) It is like an abrogated ruling and cannot be acted upon.
C) It may be used if the later opinion is unknown.
D) It is still valid but less preferred.

Key Takeaway
A mujtahid's retracted opinion is abrogated; only his final opinion constitutes the madhhab for fatwa.

Hierarchy of Sources and Types of Tasheeh
Ruling on prioritizing mutun, shuruh, fatawa and tasheeh types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi hierarchy of source texts (mutun, shuruh, fatawa) to determine the mufta bihi ruling and distinguish between implicit correction (tasheeh iltizami) and explicit correction (tasheeh sarih).

Core Idea
Mutun override shuruh and fatawa; explicit correction overrides implicit correction.

In Hanafi ifta, not all sources carry equal weight. This passage from Rasm al-Mufti lays down a clear hierarchy: the core matn texts (mutun) are primary, followed by commentaries (shuruh), and finally later fatwa compilations. It also introduces an important distinction between implicit correction (the assumption that inclusion in a reliable matn indicates correctness) and explicit correction (a later scholar's express declaration). Understanding this hierarchy ensures that the mufti relies on the most authoritative layers of the madhhab.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three tiers of source texts
List the categories: mutun (e.g., al-Hidayah, al-Quduri), shuruh (e.g., al-Bahr al-Ra'iq), and fatawa compilations (e.g., al-Fatawa al-Hindiyya). They are ranked in descending order of authority.
The hierarchy is: mutun > shuruh > fatawa.
2
Define implicit correction (tasheeh iltizami)
Understand that the authors of mutun committed themselves to transmitting only the correct ruling, so simply being in a matn gives a ruling an implicit correction.
This correction is inherent, not stated explicitly.
3
Define explicit correction (tasheeh sarih)
This is a direct statement from a later scholar (e.g., Allama Qasim in his 'Tasheeh') that a particular ruling is correct.
Explicit correction overrides implicit correction because it is a deliberate, express declaration.
4
Apply the priority rule in a conflict scenario
If a matn ruling conflicts with a fatwa book, the matn prevails. If a shuruh ruling conflicts with a fatwa book, the shuruh prevails. If an explicit correction conflicts with an implicit correction, the explicit correction prevails.
This guards against relying on later compilations that may be less accurate.
5
Recognize the practical outcome
The mufta bihi (operative ruling) is determined by the highest authoritative source that has not been overridden by an explicit correction.
Use the example from the passage: testimony of a blind person is invalid because the mutun agree on it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يقدم المتون ثم الشروح ثم الفتاوى
التاسعة ما ذكره العلامة قاسم في "تصحيحه ": "أن ما في المتون مصحح تصحيحا التزاميا، والتصحيح الصريح مقدم على التصحيح الالتزامي".
قلت حاصله أن أصحاب المتون التزموا وضع القول الصحيح، فيكون ما في غيرها مقابل الصحيح، ما لم يصرح بتصحيحه، فيقدم عليها؛ لأنه تصحيح صريح، فيقدم على التصحيح الالتزامي. وفي شهادات "الخيرية" في جواب سؤال: المذهب الصحيح المفتى به الذي مشت عليه أصحاب المتون الموضوعة لنقل الصحيح من المذهب الذي هو ظاهر الرواية أن شهادة الأعمى لا تصح". ثم قال: وحيث علم أن القول هو الذي تواردت عليه المتون فهو المعتمد المعمول به؛ إذ صرحوا بأنه إذا تعارض ما في المتون والفتاوى، فالمعتمد ما في وكذا يقدم ما في الشروح على ما في الفتاوى". وفي فصل الحبس من "البحر": "والعمل على ما في المتون؛ لأنه إذا تعارض ما في المتون والفتاوى فالمعتمد ما في المتون، كما في "أنفع الوسائل"، وكذا يقدم ما في الشروح على ما في الفتاوى". أي لما صرح به في "أنفع الوسائل" أيضا في مسألة قسمة الوقف حيث قال: "لا يفتى بنقول الفتاوى، بل نقول الفتاوى إنما يستأنس بها إذا لم يوجد ما يعارضها من كتب الأصول ونقل المذهب، أما مع وجود غيرها المتون، لا يلتفت إليها خصوصا إذا لم يكن نص فيها على الفتوى".
The mutun (matn texts) are given precedence, then the shuruh (commentaries), then the fatawa (fatwa books).
The ninth: what Allama Qasim mentioned in his 'Tasheeh': 'What is in the mutun is corrected with an implicit correction (tasheeh iltizami), and explicit correction (tasheeh sarih) is given precedence over implicit correction'.
I say: Its summary is that the authors of the mutun committed to placing the correct statement, so what is in other books is opposed to the correct, unless it is explicitly corrected; then it is given precedence over them because it is an explicit correction, which is given precedence over implicit correction. And in 'Shahadat' of 'al-Khairiyya' in answer to a question: The correct madhhab, which is the relied-upon fatwa, upon which the authors of the mutun, composed to transmit the correct from the madhhab which is zahir al-riwayah, have proceeded, is that the testimony of a blind person is not valid. Then he said: And when it is known that the statement is that which the mutun have agreed upon, it is the relied-upon and acted-upon; for they have explicitly stated that if there is conflict between what is in the mutun and the fatawa, the relied-upon is what is in ... and likewise the shuruh are given precedence over the fatawa. And in the chapter on habs (endowment) from 'al-Bahr': 'Practice is according to what is in the mutun; because if what is in the mutun and the fatawa conflict, the relied-upon is what is in the mutun, as in 'Anfa' al-Wasa'il', and likewise the shuruh are given precedence over the fatawa'. That is, as was explicitly stated in 'Anfa' al-Wasa'il' also in the issue of division of waqf where he said: 'Fatwa is not given based on the statements of fatawa books; rather, the statements of fatawa books are only used for support if there is nothing opposing them from the books of fundamentals and transmission of the madhhab. But when there exists other [i.e., mutun], they are not considered, especially if they do not contain an explicit fatwa.'
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage establishes the hierarchy of sources in Hanafi ifta: mutun (core texts) take precedence over shuruh (commentaries), which in turn take precedence over later fatawa compilations. It introduces the distinction between implicit correction (tasheeh iltizami), where the sheer inclusion in a reliable matn indicates correctness, and explicit correction (tasheeh sarih), where a later scholar expressly declares a ruling correct. The latter overrides the former. This is illustrated with specific examples: in 'al-Khairiyya', the testimony of a blind person is ruled invalid because the mutun agree on it; in 'al-Bahr', the principle is reiterated, and 'Anfa' al-Wasa'il' warns against relying solely on fatawa books when mutun exist. The rule guards against giving undue weight to fatwa compilations when they disagree with foundational texts.

Key Terms
التصحيح الصريح (al-tasheeh al-sarih): An express declaration by a later scholar that a particular ruling is correct
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwayah): The transmitted rulings from Imam Abu Hanifa and his principal students via reliable chains
التصحيح الالتزامي (al-tasheeh al-iltizami): Implicit correction: the presumption that the rulings in a reliable matn are correct because the author committed to transmitting only the correct view.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When there is a conflict between a ruling in a matn (like al-Hidayah) and a later fatwa compilation, which is given precedence according to this passage?
A) The matn is given precedence.
B) The later fatwa is given precedence because it is newer.
C) They are equally balanced; the mufti may choose.
D) The shuruh are given precedence.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi mufti must always give precedence to the mutun over later works, and treat explicit correction as overriding implicit correction.

Fatwa Books as Personal Selections, Not Authoritative Madhhab
Clarifying that fatwa books contain personal selections, not authoritative madhhab

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between fatwa compilations (ikhtiyarat al-mashayikh) and the authoritative books of the Hanafi madhhab, and apply the rule that fatwa books do not override core texts.

Core Idea
Fatwa books record personal selections, not transmitted madhhab; they cannot oppose the mutun and zahir al-riwayah.

This lesson presents a critical refinement from Sadr al-Din Sulayman, transmitted through Shams al-Din al-Hariri. He explains that later fatwa compilations represent the choices of individual scholars, not the school's transmitted position. This distinction is vital for a mufti: it tells us which sources are primary and which are secondary guidance. Without this, one might mistakenly treat a compiler's preference as the madhhab itself.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Key Claim
What does Sadr al-Din Sulayman say about fatwa books? Highlight the exact phrase: 'These fatwa books are the selections (ikhtiyarat) of the mashayikh.'
2
Distinguish the Two Categories
Differentiate between 'books of the madhhab' (kutub al-madhhab) and fatwa compilations. The former are the transmitted core texts; the latter are later personal choices.
Books of the madhhab include the mutun and zahir al-riwayah works.
3
Understand the Implication
What is the practical consequence? If a fatwa book appears to contradict a madhhab book, the madhhab book holds authority. Fatwa books are not evidence of contradiction but of the compiler's preference.
This prevents a mufti from mistakenly overruling primary sources with secondary ones.
4
Apply to Real Fatwa Practice
When consulting a fatwa compilation, a mufti must recognize it as a guide to later scholarly opinion, not as an independent source that can overrule the mutun.
The mufta bihi is still determined by the core texts unless a later tarjih explicitly reinterprets them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ورأيت في بعض كتب المتأخرين نقلاً عن " إيضاح الاستدلال على إبطال الاستبدال" لقاضي القضاة شمس الدين الحريري، أحد شراح "الهداية": "أن صدر الدين سليمان قال: إن هذه الفتاوى هي اختيارات المشايخ فلا تعارض كتب المذهب. قال: وكذا كان يقول غيره من مشايخنا، وبه أقول."
And I saw in some books of the later scholars a transmission from 'Idah al-Istidlal 'ala Ibtal al-Istibdal' by Qadi al-Qudat Shams al-Din al-Hariri, one of the commentators of al-Hidayah: 'That Sadr al-Din Sulayman said: These fatwa books are the selections (ikhtiyarat) of the mashayikh, so they do not oppose the books of the madhhab. He said: And others of our mashayikh used to say likewise, and that is what I say.'
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This short paragraph is a crucial refinement of the previous rule. Sadr al-Din Sulayman explains that fatwa compilations (like al-Fatawa al-Hindiyya or al-Khaniyya) are not straightforward transmissions of the madhhab; they represent the personal selections (ikhtiyarat) of the scholars who compiled them. Therefore, if a ruling in a fatwa book contradicts the established books of the madhhab (like the mutun of zahir al-riwayah), it is the madhhab books that are authoritative. The fatwa books are not to be taken as contradictory evidence but as guidance on which opinion the compiler preferred. This reinforces the principle that mutun and other core texts are the primary reference.

Key Terms
اختيارات المشايخ (ikhtiyarat al-mashayikh): Personal preferences of later scholars, not authoritative source of the madhhab.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) They overrule older texts because they reflect later ijtihad.
D) They are equal to the matn texts.

Key Takeaway
Fatwa books are guides to later scholars' preferences; the core madhhab texts remain the primary authority.

Reliable Mutun and Precedence Among Fatwa Books
Listing reliable mutun and a rule about fatwa book precedence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the seven reliable mutun of the Hanafi madhhab and apply the rule of precedence among fatwa books based on the presence of illah and Qadi Khan's methodology.

Core Idea
Only seven mutun transmit the zahir al-riwayah; fatawa books like al-Khaniyya take precedence when they include an explicit illah.

In this lesson, we examine the canon of reliable matn texts that form the backbone of Hanafi fatwa. The learned author specifies which matns are mu'tabara and then introduces a crucial rule: when multiple opinions exist, the one accompanied by an illah (legal cause) is given precedence. This principle is exemplified in the Fatawa of Qadi Khan, whose methodology of selecting the most apparent opinions makes his work authoritative.

Guided Path
1
List the seven reliable mutun
Memorize these names: al-Bidaya, Mukhtasar al-Quduri, al-Mukhtar, al-Nuqaya, al-Wiqaya, al-Kanz, al-Multaqa.
These are based on zahir al-riwayah.
2
Distinguish reliable mutun from others
Understand that matns like al-Ghurar and al-Tanwir contain many fatwa-based issues, so they are not at the same level.
This distinction affects which texts are primary for fatwa.
3
State the precedence rule for fatwa books
When opinions conflict, the one in Fatawa Qadi Khan or Multaqa al-Abhur has precedence, especially if it provides an illah.
The rule includes that a ruling with an illah is preferred over one without.
4
Apply the rule to a hypothetical scenario
Given two opinions, one with illah from al-Khaniyya and one without from another source, adopt the opinion with the illah.
This is part of the mufti's methodology.

And I began with that which is the most famous; in response to seekers and to facilitate for those who desire.' And likewise...

Commentary
This lesson specifies which mutun are considered mu'tabara (reliable) for transmitting the madhhab: al-Bidaya, Mukhtasar al-Quduri, al-Mukhtar, al-Nuqaya, al-Wiqaya, al-Kanz, and al-Multaqa. These are composed from the zahir al-riwayah texts. In contrast, matns like al-Ghurar and al-Tanwir contain many fatwa-based issues and thus are not at the same level. The passage then introduces a poetic rule (couplet) about precedence among fatwa books: the earlier statements in al-Khaniyya (Qadi Khan's fatawa) and Multaqa al-Abhur have preference; when the source provides an illah (ratio legis) for one opinion and ignores another, the one with the illah is followed. It concludes with Qadi Khan's own statement that he selected the most apparent and famous opinions, which is why his fatawa are given priority.

Key Terms
متون المعتبرة (al-mutun al-mu'tabara): The core matn texts that reliably transmit the Hanafi madhhab from zahir al-riwayah
العلة (al-illah): The legal cause or ratio legis behind a ruling

Key Takeaway

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa

Authoritative Texts and Prioritization of Opinions in Hanafi Ifta
Explains which texts are relied upon and how to prioritize conflicting opinions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the authoritative Hanafi texts (al-Mutun al-Mu'tabara) and apply the rules of tarjih based on authorial method, evidence delay, and reasoning to determine the mufta bihi opinion.

Core Idea
Not all Hanafi books are equal — only texts from Zahir al-Riwayah are primary; later collections require careful hierarchy.

This lesson unpacks the foundational methodology for Hanafi fatwa: knowing which texts to rely on and how to resolve apparent conflicts between different opinions. The passage from Sharh 'Uqud Rasm al-Mufti lays out a systematic approach — distinguishing between primary sources and later compilations, and applying specific rules of preponderance (tarjih). By the end, you will be able to classify Hanafi texts and use the principles of delay, reasoning, and authorial commitment to identify the operative opinion for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the authoritative texts (al-Mutun al-Mu'tabara)
List the texts explicitly mentioned as relied-upon for transmitting the madhhab from Zahir al-Riwayah: al-Bidaya, Mukhtasar al-Quduri, al-Mukhtar, al-Niqaya, al-Wiqaya, al-Kanz, al-Multaqa. Note the distinguishing criterion: they must be based on Zahir al-Riwayah.
Contrast with al-Ghurar and al-Tanwir, which contain many fatwa-based issues and are not primary.
2
Apply the rule of precedence in fatwa collections
In collections like Fatawa Qadi Khan and Multaqa al-Abhar, the author explicitly states his method: he presents the most apparent or most relied-upon opinion first. Follow that opinion unless evidence indicates otherwise.
The passage quotes Qadi Khan's preface: 'I put forward what is most apparent and begin with the most famous.'
3
Apply the rule of evidence delay
In books that mention multiple opinions with their evidences (e.g., al-Hidayah and its commentaries), the custom is to delay the Imam's opinion, then mention each opinion's evidence, and finally mention the Imam's evidence including responses. This order indicates preponderance for the Imam unless they explicitly prefer another.
Ibn al-Shilbi confirms: giving Imam's evidence precedence and answering opponents is an indication of practice on his saying.
4
Apply the rule of reasoned opinion (ta'lil)
When two opinions are mentioned and one is given a reason ('illa) while the other is not, the reasoned opinion is preponderant. Mentioning the cause shows care and encouragement.
This is supported by al-Khayr al-Ramli and the principle from al-Tahrir: the ruling with mentioned cause is preponderant.
5
Interpret the verse and Nasafi's rule
The poetic verse states that in al-Khaniyya and Multaqa, earlier opinions have merit; in other books, rely on what they delayed. Imam al-Nasafi's rule from al-Mustasfa: among three opinions, the preponderant is the first or last, not the middle. But qualify: if the book's custom is known, follow it; if evidences are mentioned, the last is preferred.
This clarifies the need to know the author's method in each book.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تقسيم التصحيح الالتزامي
شرح عقود رسم المفتي ورأيت في بعض كتب المتأخرين نقلاً عن " إيضاح الاستدلال على إبطال الاستبدال" لقاضي القضاة شمس الدين الحريري، أحد شراح "الهداية": "أن صدر الدين سليمان قال: إن هذه الفتاوى هي اختيارات المشايخ فلا تعارض كتب المذهب. قال: وكذا كان يقول غيره من مشايخنا، وبه أقول.
[المتون المعتبرة]

شرح عقود رسم المفتي
٦١
تقسيم التصحيح الالتزامي
صاحب "ملتقى الأبحر" التزم تقديم القول المعتمد. وما عداهما الكتب التي تذكر فيها الأقوال بأدلتها كـ"الهداية" من وشروحها. وشروح "الكنز"، و"كافي النسفي"، و"البدائع" وغيرها من الكتب المبسوطة، فقد جرت العادة فيها عند حكاية الأقوال أنهم يؤخرون قول الإمام، ثم يذكرون دليل كل قول، ثم يذكرون دليل الإمام متضمنا للجواب عما استدل به غيره. وهذا ترجيح له إلا أن ينصوا على ترجيح غيره.
خالفه
قال شيخ الإسلام العلامة ابن الشلبي في فتاواه: "الأصل أن العمل على قول أبي حنيفة، ولذا ترجح المشايخ دليله في الأغلب على دليل من أصحابه، ويجيبون عما استدل به مخالفه، وهذا أمارة العمل من بقوله، وإن لم يصرحوا بالفتوى عليه؛ إذ الترجيح كصريح التصحيح". وفي آخر المستصفى للإمام النسفي إذا ذكر في المسألة ثلاثة أقوال، فالراجح هو الأول أو الأخير لا الوسط. قلت وينبغي تقييده بما إذا لم تعلم عادة صاحب ذلك الكتاب، و لم يذكر الأدلة. أما إذا علمت كما مر عن "الخانية" و"الملتقى" فتتبع، وأما إذا ذكرت الأدلة، فالمرجح الأخير كما قلنا.
وكذا لو ذكروا قولين مثلا وعللوا لأحدهما كان ترجيحا له على غير المعلل، كما أفاده الخير الرملي في كتاب الغصب من فتاواه "الخيرية". ونظيره ما في " "التحرير" وشرحه في فصل الترجيح في المتعارضين: "أن الحكم الذي تعرض فيه للعلة يترجح على الحكم الذي لم يتعرض فيه
لها؛ لأن ذكر علته يدل على الاهتمام به والحث عليه".
Division of Obligatory Correction
Commentary on 'Uqud Rasm al-Mufti: I saw in some later books, quoting from 'Idah al-Istidlal 'ala Ibtal al-Istibdal' by Qadi al-Qudah Shams al-Din al-Hariri, one of the commentators of al-Hidaya: 'That Sadr al-Din Sulayman said: These fatawa are the choices of the mashayikh, so they do not contradict the books of the madhhab. He said: And likewise other mashayikh of ours used to say, and I say the same.'
[The Relied-Upon Texts]
Then it is not hidden that what is meant by 'al-Mutun' are the relied-upon texts like al-Bidaya, Mukhtasar al-Quduri, al-Mukhtar, al-Niqaya, al-Wiqaya, al-Kanz, and al-Multaqa; for they are composed to transmit the madhhab based on what is from al-Zahir al-Riwayah. Unlike the texts al-Ghurar by Mulla Khusraw and al-Tanwir by al-Tamartashi al-Ghazzi; for in them are many fatwa-based issues. And the precedence of the sayings in al-Khaniyya and Multaqa al-Abhar has merit, and in other than them rely on what they delayed its evidence. Likewise, when they have explained one (saying) and neglected the explanation of the other.
Division of Obligatory Correction:
That is, the first of the sayings occurring in the Fatawa of Imam Qadi Khan has merit over others in preponderance, because he said at the beginning of the Fatawa: 'In matters where the later scholars had many sayings, I restricted myself to one or two sayings, and I put forward what is the most apparent. And I began with what is the most famous, answering the seekers and making it easy for the desirous.' And likewise the author of Multaqa al-Abhar committed to presenting the relied-upon saying. As for books other than these two, which mention sayings with their evidences like al-Hidaya and its commentaries, and commentaries of al-Kanz, Kafi al-Nasafi, al-Bada'i', and other extended books, the custom in them when relating sayings is that they delay the saying of the Imam, then mention the evidence of each saying, then mention the evidence of the Imam including the response to what others used as evidence. This is a preponderance for him unless they explicitly state preponderance for another.
Shaykh al-Islam 'Allamah Ibn al-Shilbi said in his Fatawa: 'The principle is that practice is upon the saying of Abu Hanifa, and therefore the mashayikh give preponderance to his evidence in most cases over the evidence of his companions, and they respond to what his opponent used as evidence. This is an indication of practice upon his saying, even if they do not explicitly state the fatwa upon it; for preponderance is like explicit correction.' And at the end of al-Mustasfa of Imam al-Nasafi: 'If three sayings are mentioned in a matter, the preponderant is the first or the last, not the middle.' I say: It should be restricted to when the custom of the author of that book is not known, and evidences are not mentioned. But if it is known, as passed regarding al-Khaniyya and al-Multaqa, then follow it. And if evidences are mentioned, then the last is the preponderant as we said.
Likewise, if they mention two sayings, for example, and give a reason for one of them, that is a preponderance for it over the one not given a reason, as al-Khayr al-Ramli mentioned in the chapter on Ghasp in his Fatawa 'al-Khayriyya'. And similar is found in al-Tahrir and its commentary in the chapter on preponderance regarding conflicting evidence: 'The ruling in which the cause is mentioned is preponderant over the ruling in which it is not mentioned, because mentioning its cause indicates care for it and encouragement of it.'
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage from Sharh 'Uqud Rasm al-Mufti presents a methodological discussion on how to determine the relied-upon position in Hanafi fatwa. It begins by citing Sadr al-Din Sulayman's view that fatwa collections represent the choices of later scholars and do not contradict the foundational madhhab texts. The author then clarifies which texts are considered the reliable 'متون' for transmitting the madhhab—those based on Zahir al-Riwayah, such as al-Bidaya, Mukhtasar al-Quduri, al-Mukhtar, al-Niqaya, al-Wiqaya, al-Kanz, and al-Multaqa. Texts like al-Ghurar and al-Tanwir are distinguished as containing many fatwa-based issues, not pure Zahir al-Riwayah. A poetic couplet summarizes the hierarchy: in the Khaniyya and Multaqa, earlier opinions have precedence; in other books that present evidence, one should follow the opinion whose evidence is detailed last, as that indicates preponderance. The passage further explains that authors like Qadi Khan and the author of Multaqa al-Abhar explicitly state their method of prioritizing the most apparent or most relied-upon opinion. In books that mention evidence, such as al-Hidaya and its commentaries, the custom is to delay the Imam's opinion and then give his evidence, which implicitly preponderates his view unless otherwise stated. Ibn al-Shilbi confirms the principle that practice is on Abu Hanifa's saying, supported by the mashayikh's tendency to give his evidence precedence and answer opponents. A rule from Imam al-Nasafi's al-Mustasfa states that among three opinions, the first or last is preponderant, not the middle, but this is qualified: if the book's custom is known (as in Khaniyya and Multaqa), follow it; if evidences are mentioned, the last is preferred. Finally, if one opinion is given a reason (تعليل) and another is not, the reasoned opinion is preponderant, as explained by al-Khayr al-Ramli and in al-Tahrir. This passage underscores that Hanafi ifta is not arbitrary but follows a disciplined hierarchy of texts and reasoning.

Key Terms
المتون المعتبرة (al-Mutun al-Mu'tabara): The authoritative texts that reliably transmit the Hanafi madhhab from the Zahir al-Riwayah corpus.
ظاهر الرواية (Zahir al-Riwayah): The six core books of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani (al-Mabsut, al-Jami' al-Saghir, etc.) which form the authoritative basis of the madhhab.
ترجيح (Tarjih): The process of giving preponderance to one opinion over another based on textual evidence, authorial method, or other indicators.
التصحيح الالتزامي (al-Tashih al-Iltizami): The obligatory correction and classification of texts and opinions that a mufti must adhere to.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following books is considered a 'متن معتبر' for transmitting the relied-upon positions of the Hanafi madhhab?
A) متن الغرر لملا خسرو
B) متن التنوير للتمرتاشي الغزي
C) متن الكنز
D) فتاواى قاضي خان

Key Takeaway
Hanafi ifta follows a disciplined hierarchy: primary texts from Zahir al-Riwayah, then fatwa collections with explicit methods, then evidence-presenting works where delay and reasoning indicate preponderance.

Identifying the Relied-Upon Opinion in Hanafi Books
Classifying books into three levels and determining the relied-upon opinion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rasm al-mufti rules to identify the mufta bihi opinion from the structure of fiqh texts, including classification of books, order of sayings, and presence of proofs or causal explanations.

Core Idea
In Hanafi books that cite proofs, the delayed saying of Abu Hanifa with his proof is the mufta bihi.

Not every opinion cited in a Hanafi fiqh book is equally authoritative. The later jurists developed a system to read the books: the way a saying is presented—its position, its proof, and whether a cause is given—indicates its weight. This lesson equips you to decode that system, drawing from the works of al-Hidayah, al-Kanz, and the rulings of multipliers like Ibn al-Shalabi and al-Nasafi.

Guided Path
1
Classify the book type
First, determine whether the book is a concise mukhtasar that commits to the correct opinion (like Multaqa al-Abhar) or a detailed work that cites proofs (like al-Hidayah). The rule differs: in the former, the stated opinion is directly mufta bihi; in the latter, you must analyze the presentation.
Books like al-Quduri also fall into the concise category.
2
Analyze the custom in detailed books
In books like al-Hidayah, the custom (adah) is to delay the saying of Imam Abu Hanifa, then mention the proofs of all positions, then present Abu Hanifa's proof including responses to opponents. This delay is a form of tarjih (preference) for his view.
This custom overrides the default assumption that Abu Hanifa's view is always preferred.
3
Apply the default weighting rule
Unless the author explicitly states they are following another view, the delayed saying (the last mentioned) with its full proof is the relied-upon opinion. If multiple sayings are mentioned without proofs, the rule from al-Nasafi applies: the first or last is preferred, not the middle.
This default rule applies only when the book's custom is unknown and proofs are not mentioned.
4
Identify additional indicators
If one saying is given a causal explanation (ta'lil) or if its proof is presented with a response to opponents, that strengthens its preference. The mention of a cause indicates concern for that ruling.
This is explicitly drawn from al-Tahrir and Fatawa al-Khayriyyah.
5
Resolve intra-Hanafi disagreements
Note the objection of Ibn al-Shalabi: he argues that the very habit of scholars giving preference to Abu Hanifa's proof (even without explicit fatwa) is an amarah that his saying is relied upon. The passage accepts this but adds the qualification about the last-mentioned proof.
This nuance helps avoid confusion when a book does not follow the typical custom.

Source Text
