تقسيم التصحيح الالتزامي
صاحب "ملتقى الأبحر" التزم تقديم القول المعتمد. وما عداهما الكتب التي تذكر فيها الأقوال بأدلتها كـ"الهداية" من وشروحها. وشروح "الكنز"، و"كافي النسفي"، و"البدائع" وغيرها من الكتب المبسوطة، فقد جرت العادة فيها عند حكاية الأقوال أنهم يؤخرون قول الإمام، ثم يذكرون دليل كل قول، ثم يذكرون دليل الإمام متضمنا للجواب عما استدل به غيره. وهذا ترجيح له إلا أن ينصوا على ترجيح غيره.
خالفه
قال شيخ الإسلام العلامة ابن الشلبي في فتاواه: "الأصل أن العمل على قول أبي حنيفة، ولذا ترجح المشايخ دليله في الأغلب على دليل من أصحابه، ويجيبون عما استدل به مخالفه، وهذا أمارة العمل من بقوله، وإن لم يصرحوا بالفتوى عليه؛ إذ الترجيح كصريح التصحيح". وفي آخر المستصفى للإمام النسفي إذا ذكر في المسألة ثلاثة أقوال، فالراجح هو الأول أو الأخير لا الوسط. قلت وينبغي تقييده بما إذا لم تعلم عادة صاحب ذلك الكتاب، و لم يذكر الأدلة. أما إذا علمت كما مر عن "الخانية" و"الملتقى" فتتبع، وأما إذا ذكرت الأدلة، فالمرجح الأخير كما قلنا.
وكذا لو ذكروا قولين مثلا وعللوا لأحدهما كان ترجيحا له على غير المعلل، كما أفاده الخير الرملي في كتاب الغصب من فتاواه "الخيرية". ونظيره ما في " "التحرير" وشرحه في فصل الترجيح في المتعارضين: "أن الحكم الذي تعرض فيه للعلة يترجح على الحكم الذي لم يتعرض فيه لها؛ لأن ذكر علته يدل على الاهتمام به والحث عليه".
Classification of Compulsory Correction: The author of Multaqa al-Abhar committed to presenting the relied-upon saying. Apart from these two [types], the books that mention sayings with their proofs, like al-Hidayah and its commentaries, and commentaries of al-Kanz, Kafi al-Nasafi, al-Bada'i' and other expansive books, the custom in them when relating sayings is that they delay the saying of Imam [Abu Hanifa], then they mention the proof of each saying, then they mention the proof of the Imam, including the response to what others used as evidence. This is a weighting for him unless they explicitly state a weighting for someone else. He disagreed: Shaykh al-Islam Ibn al-Shalabi said in his Fatawa: 'The principle is that practice is on the saying of Abu Hanifa, and therefore the scholars give preference to his proof in most cases over the proof of his companions, and they respond to what his opponent used as evidence. This is an indication of acting by his saying, even if they do not explicitly issue the fatwa upon it; for weighting is like explicit correction.' And at the end of al-Mustasfa by Imam al-Nasafi: if three sayings are mentioned in a case, the preponderant is the first or the last, not the middle. I say: It should be restricted to when the custom of the author of that book is not known, and the proofs are not mentioned. However, if it is known as mentioned earlier about al-Khaniyah and Multaqa, then it is followed; and if the proofs are mentioned, then the preponderant is the last as we said. Similarly, if they mention two sayings for example and give a cause for one, it is a preference for it over the one not given a cause, as al-Khayr al-Ramli conveyed in the book of Ghasb from his Fatawa al-Khayriyah. And its parallel is in al-Tahrir and its commentary in the chapter of preference between conflicting matters: 'A ruling for which a cause is mentioned takes precedence over a ruling for which no cause is mentioned, because mentioning its cause indicates concern for it and encouragement of it.'
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage is a methodological rule (rasm al-mufti) about how to identify the relied-upon opinion (al-qawl al-mu'tamad) in Hanafi fiqh books. The author first classifies books into three types: (1) those like Mukhtasar al-Quduri and Multaqa al-Abhar (not explicitly mentioned but implied by 'the two'), which are concise and commit to presenting the correct opinion; (2) books that present sayings with their proofs, such as al-Hidayah, its commentaries, etc.; (3) expansive books. In the second type, the custom is to delay the saying of Imam Abu Hanifa, then mention proofs of all, then Abu Hanifa's proof including responses to opponents. This delay itself is a preference (tarjih) for his view unless they explicitly prefer another. Ibn al-Shalabi objects: the original principle is to act on Abu Hanifa's saying, and the scholars' practice of giving his proof preference is an indication of acting on it, even without explicit fatwa. Then al-Nasafi's rule: for three sayings, the first or last is preponderant, not the middle. The author qualifies: this applies only when the author's custom is unknown and proofs absent; if known (like al-Khaniyah and Multaqa), follow that; if proofs mentioned, the last is preponderant. Finally, if a cause is given for one saying, that indicates preference.

Key Terms
تقسيم التصحيح الالتزامي (Taqsim al-Tashih al-Iltizami): Classification of books according to their commitment to presenting the correct (sahih) opinion.
الأمارة (al-Amarah): An indication or sign; here it refers to the implied preference shown by the way proofs are presented.
الترجيح (al-Tarjih): The act of weighting one opinion over another based on evidence or custom.
العلة (al-'Illah): The effective cause or rationale given for a ruling; mentioning it indicates preference.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the rule when three sayings are mentioned without proofs and the author's custom is unknown?
A) The middle saying is always the correct one.
B) The first or last saying is preferred, not the middle.
C) All three are equally valid until a later tarjih appears.
D) The first saying is the mufta bihi because it comes first.

Key Takeaway
The relied-upon opinion in Hanafi books with proofs is typically the last mention with full proof, but this is overridden by the author's known custom or explicit tarjih.

Strength of Fatwa Indicators: Sahih vs Asah
Examining the relative strength of fatwa indicator terms with a critical refinement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the standard hierarchy of fatwa indicator terms and apply the correct ruling when conflicting corrections between 'sahih' and 'asah' occur.

Core Idea
Al-asah is normally stronger than al-sahih, but when two imams conflict, the one saying al-sahih is preferred.

The Hanafi tradition uses a set of terms to indicate the relied-upon opinion for fatwa. Among them, 'sahih' and 'asah' are common, but their relative strength is not always straightforward. This passage from Sharh 'Uqud Rasm al-Mufti presents the standard hierarchy and then introduces a critical refinement: in cases of conflicting corrections, the term 'sahih' takes precedence over 'asah'. Understanding this nuance is essential for a mufti.

Guided Path
1
Identify standard hierarchy
List the expressions for fatwa indication from the passage. Note that in the standard list from al-Fatawa al-Khayriyah and al-Mudmarat, 'al-asah' is stated as more emphatic than 'al-sahih'.
This is the baseline rule that a mufti typically learns first.
2
Recognize the conflict
Find the report from Sharh al-Munyah that contradicts the standard hierarchy. It states that when two reliable imams correct a mas'alah, one using 'sahih' and the other 'asah', the opinion of the one who said 'sahih' is given precedence.
This introduces a practical exception to the baseline rule.
3
Analyze the basis
Consider why 'al-sahih' might be preferred in conflicting corrections: it is a direct assertion of correctness, whereas 'al-asah' is comparative and may imply uncertainty.
The passage does not fully explain, but the preference suggests that direct correction (sahih) is more decisive than a comparative correction.
4
Derive the application
Conclude that a mufti should generally consider 'al-asah' stronger, but when confronted with two conflicting corrections using these terms, he should follow the one using 'al-sahih'.
This nuance protects the mufti from erroneously relying on the weaker opinion in practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هل الصحيح أكد أم الأصح؟
شرح عقود رسم المفتي وحيثما وجدت قولين وقد صُحح واحد فذاك المعتمد بنحو ذا الفتوى عليه الأشبه والأظهر المختار ذا والأوجه أو الصحيح، والأصح أكد منه وقيل: عكسه المؤكد كذا به يُفتى عليه الفتوى وذان من جميع تلك أقوى [العلامات للإفتاء]
قال في آخر "الفتاوى الخيرية" وفي أول "المضمرات": "أما العلامات للإفتاء فقوله وعليه الفتوى وبه يفتى وبه نأخذ، وعليه الاعتماد، وعليه عمل اليوم، وعليه عمل الأمة، وهو الصحيح، وهو الأصح، الأظهر، وهو المختار في زماننا، وفتوى مشايخنا، وهو الأشبه، وهو الأوجه وغيرها من الألفاظ المذكورة في متن هذا الكتاب في وهو محل صدر، في حاشية البزدوي. من لفظ وبعض هذه الألفاظ أكد من بعض فلفظ "الفتوى" أكد الصحيح، والأصح والأشبه وغيرها. ولفظ "به يفتي" أكد من لفظ "الفتوى عليه". والأصح أكد من "الصحيح". والأحوط أكد من "الاحتياط".
هل الصحيح أكد أم الأصح؟]
لكن في "شرح المنية" في بحث مس المصحف والذي أخذناه من المشايخ أنه إذا تعارض إمامان معتبران في التصحيح، فقال أحدهما: الصحيح كذا، وقال الآخر: الأصح كذا، فالأخذ بقول من قال: "الصحيح" أولى من الأخذ بقول من قال: "الأصح "؛ لأن الصحيح
Is 'sahih' more emphatic or 'asah'? Sharh Uqud Rasm al-Mufti: Whenever you find two sayings and one has been corrected (suhhiha), that is the relied-upon, such as: 'al-fatwa upon it', 'al-ashbah', 'al-azhar', 'al-mukhtar', 'al-audah' or 'al-sahih'. And 'al-asah' is more emphatic than it, and it is said: its opposite is the emphatic? this is how fatwa is given? 'al-fatwa upon it' and these two are strongest of all [indicators for fatwa]. It is said at the end of al-Fatawa al-Khayriyah and at the beginning of al-Mudmarat: 'As for the indicators for fatwa: his saying: 'and upon it the fatwa', 'and by it fatwa is given', 'and by it we take', 'and upon it reliance', 'and upon it the practice today', 'and upon it the practice of the ummah', 'and it is the correct (al-sahih)', 'and it is the more correct (al-asah)', 'al-azhar', 'and it is the chosen in our time', 'and the fatwa of our scholars', 'and it is the most similar (al-ashbah)', 'and it is the most appropriate (al-audah)', and other expressions mentioned in the text of this book in its place, in the margin of al-Bazdawi. Some of these expressions are more emphatic than others: the word 'al-fatwa' is more emphatic than 'al-sahih', 'al-asah', 'al-ashbah' and others. And the word 'bihi yufata' is more emphatic than the word 'al-fatwa alayh'. And 'al-asah' is more emphatic than 'al-sahih'. And 'al-ahwat' is more emphatic than 'al-ihtiyat'. Is 'sahih' more emphatic or 'asah'? However, in Sharh al-Munyah in the discussion of touching the mus-haf, and what we took from the scholars: that if two reliable imams conflict in correction, one says: 'al-sahih is such' and the other says: 'al-asah is such', then acting by the one who said 'al-sahih' is preferable to acting by the one who said 'al-asah'; because 'al-sahih'...
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage is a methodological refinement (critical_refinement) about the hierarchy of terms used to indicate the fatwa-worthy opinion. It starts by listing common expressions of tarjih: 'al-fatwa 'alayh', 'al-ashbah', 'al-azhar', 'al-mukhtar', 'al-audah', 'al-sahih', 'al-asah', etc. Then it quotes a standard hierarchy from al-Fatawa al-Khayriyah and al-Mudmarat: 'al-fatwa' is strongest, then 'bihi yufata', then 'al-asah' is stronger than 'al-sahih', etc. However, the passage then raises a dispute: in Sharh al-Munyah, it is reported that when two imams conflict, one saying 'sahih' and the other 'asah', the opinion of the one who said 'sahih' is preferable. This contradicts the previous rule that 'asah' is stronger. The text cuts off before resolving the dispute, but it implies that in such conflicting scenarios, 'sahih' carries more weight, perhaps because 'sahih' is a direct assertion whereas 'asah' is comparative.

Key Terms
العلامات للإفتاء (al-'Alamat li al-Ifta'): Expressions used in Hanafi fiqh books to indicate the opinion that should be given as fatwa.
الصحيح (al-Sahih): The correct opinion, indicating it is the relied-upon view.
الأصح (al-Asah): The more correct opinion; comparative form of 'sahih'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relation between 'al-sahih' and 'al-asah' in the standard hierarchy?
A) 'al-sahih' is more emphatic.
B) 'al-asah' is more emphatic.
C) They are equal.
D) Neither is used for fatwa.

Key Takeaway
In the hierarchy of fatwa indicators, 'al-asah' is normally stronger, but when two imams differ in corrections, the one using 'al-sahih' is given precedence.

Hierarchy of Fatwa Indicators: al-Sahih vs. al-Asahh
Rasm al-mufti rule parsing exercise

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between binding (sahih) and discretionary (asahh) fatwa indicators and apply the practical rule from Risala Adab al-Muftiyyin.

Core Idea
Sahih binds the mufti; asahh grants choice — a single word decides the mufti's authority.

Hanafi fatwa manuals use coded phrases to indicate the relied-upon ruling. Words like 'sahih' and 'asahh' appear often, but their hierarchy is debated. This lesson unpacks the practical rule that resolves the debate: 'sahih' and its equivalents restrict the mufti, while 'asahh' and softer terms allow discretion.

Guided Path
1
Identify Key Fatwa Phrases
List all the authoritative phrases mentioned in the passage: 'sahih', 'asahh', 'alayhi al-fatwa', 'beh yufata', 'al-ma'khudh bihi', 'awla', 'arfaq', etc.
These phrases appear in Hanafi fiqh works to indicate the mu'tamad (relied-upon) position.
2
Classify Each Phrase as Binding or Discretionary
Using the rule from Risala Adab al-Muftiyyin, separate phrases that force the mufti to follow that opinion (e.g., 'sahih', 'al-ma'khudh bihi', 'beh yufata', 'alayhi al-fatwa') from those that allow choice ('asahh', 'awla', 'arfaq').
The rule states: 'If annotated with sahhih... he does not give fatwa contrary to it; if with asahh... he may give fatwa with it or its opposite.'
3
Understand the Internal Hierarchy Within Each Category
Note that some binding phrases are stronger than others: 'beh yufata' is stronger than 'alayhi al-fatwa'. Similarly, among discretionary phrases, some may be ranked, but the practical effect is the same: choice.
The passage mentions that 'beh yufata' is a stronger indicator than 'alayhi al-fatwa'.
4
Apply the Rule to a Conflict Scenario
If one reliable work says 'sahih' for a ruling and another says 'asahh' for the opposite, determine the mufti's option. According to the practical rule, he must follow the 'sahih' because it is binding, unless an exception applies.
The passage also discusses the scholarly disagreement on which term is stronger, but the practical rule settles it.
5
Memorize the Exception to the Binding Rule
When two equally reliable works each have a different ruling marked 'sahih', the mufti may choose the stronger, more suitable, or more beneficial opinion. This is the only case where 'sahih' does not bind absolutely.
Example: conflict between al-Hidaya and al-Kafi.

هل الصحيح أكد أم الأصح؟]
لكن في "شرح المنية" في بحث مس المصحف والذي أخذناه من المشايخ أنه إذا تعارض إمامان معتبران في التصحيح، فقال أحدهما: الصحيح كذا، وقال الآخر: الأصح كذا، فالأخذ بقول من قال: "الصحيح" أولى من الأخذ بقول من قال: "الأصح "; لأن الصحيح مقابله الفاسد، والأصح مقابله الصحيح، فقد وافق من قال: الأصح قائل الصحيح على أنه صحيح وأما من قال: "الصحيح" فعنده ذلك الحكم الآخر فاسد، فالأخذ بما اتفقا على أنه صحيح أولى مما هو عند أحدهما فاسد. وذكر العلامة ابن عبد الرزاق في شرحه على "الدر المختار": أن المشهور عند الجمهور أن الأصح أكد من الصحيح.
وفي شرح البيري: قال في "الطراز المذهب" ناقلا عن حاشية البزدوي: قوله: "هو الصحيح" يقتضي أن يكون غيره غير صحيح، ولفظ الأصح يقتضي أن يكون غيره صحيحا أقول: ينبغي أن يقيد ذلك بالغالب؛ لأنا وجدنا مقابل "الأصح" الرواية الشاذة، كما في شرح المجمع. وفي "الدر المختار" بعد نقله حاصل ما مر : " ثم رأيت في "رسالة آداب المفتين : إذا ذيلت رواية في كتاب معتمد رواية في كتاب معتمد بالأصح، أو الأولى، أو الأرفق، ونحوها، فله أن يفتي بها وبمخالفتها أيضا أيا شاء. وإذا ذيلت بالصحيح، أو المأخوذ به، أو به يفتى أو عليه الفتوى لم يفت مخالفها، إلا إذا كان في "الهداية" مثلا هو الصحيح، وفي "الكافي" بمخالفه هو الصحيح، فيخيّر، فيختار الأقوى عنده، والأليق، والأصلح. فليحفظ.

Is al-sahih stronger or al-asahh?
Commentary on 'Uqud Rasm al-Mufti: Whenever two statements are found and one is corrected, that is the relied-upon one, like 'the fatwa is upon it', 'the most similar', 'the most apparent', 'the chosen', 'the best', or 'sahih'. And 'asahh' is stronger than it, and it was said the opposite: the emphatic is like this, with it fatwa is given. And the fatwa upon it and that from all those are stronger. [Signs for issuing fatwa]
He said at the end of al-Fatawa al-Khairiyya and at the beginning of al-Mudmarat: 'As for the signs for issuing fatwa: his statement 'and the fatwa is upon it', 'and with it fatwa is given', 'and with it we take', 'and upon it is reliance', 'and upon it is the practice today', 'and upon it is the practice of the umma', 'and it is sahih', 'and it is asahh', 'the most apparent', 'and it is the chosen in our time', 'and the fatwa of our scholars', 'and it is the most similar', 'and it is the best', and other expressions mentioned in the text of this book in their places, in the marginalia of al-Bazdawi. And some of these expressions are stronger than others: the word 'fatwa' is stronger than sahih, asahh, ashbah, and others. And the word 'with it fatwa is given' is stronger than 'the fatwa is upon it'. And 'asahh' is stronger than 'sahih'. And 'ahtat' is stronger than 'ihtiyat'.
Is al-sahih stronger or al-asahh?]
But in Sharh al-Munya, in the discussion of touching the mushaf: and what we have taken from the scholars is that if two reliable imams conflict in correction, one saying 'sahih' and the other saying 'asahh', then accepting the one who said 'sahih' is better than accepting the one who said 'asahh'; because the opposite of sahih is corrupt, and the opposite of asahh is sahih. So the one who said asahh has agreed with the one who said sahih that it is sahih, while the one who said sahih considers that other ruling corrupt. So accepting what they agree is sahih is better than what is corrupt according to one. And Allama Ibn Abd al-Razzaq mentioned in his commentary on al-Durr al-Mukhtar: that the well-known view among the majority is that asahh is stronger than sahih.
And in Sharh al-Bayri: He said in al-Tiraz al-Madhhab, quoting from the marginalia of al-Bazdawi: His statement 'it is sahih' implies that other than it is not sahih, and the word asahh implies that other than it is sahih. I say: this should be restricted to the usual case; because we find the opposite of 'asahh' is an anomalous narration, as in Sharh al-Majma'. And in al-Durr al-Mukhtar, after quoting the gist of what passed: 'Then I saw in Risala Adab al-Muftiyyin: When a ruling in a reliable book is annotated with 'asahh', or 'awla', or 'arfaq', and the like, he may issue fatwa with it and also with the opposite whichever he wishes. And when annotated with 'sahih', or 'al-ma'khudh bihi', or 'beh yufata', or 'alayhi al-fatwa', he does not give fatwa contrary to it, unless for example in al-Hidaya it is sahih and in al-Kafi the opposite is sahih, then he chooses, and selects the stronger, more suitable, and most beneficial according to him. So let it be memorized.'
Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage is a rasm al-mufti discussion on the hierarchy of phrases used in Hanafi works to indicate the relied-upon (mu'tamad) ruling. The author begins by stating that whenever two opinions exist and one is 'corrected' (suhhiha), that is the mu'tamad. Examples of such phrases are: 'al-fatwa 'alayhi', 'al-ashbah', 'al-azhar', 'al-mukhtar', 'al-awjah', 'al-sahih'. Then he notes that 'al-asahh' is stronger (akad) than 'al-sahih', though some say the opposite. Then he lists various authoritative phrases from al-Fatawa al-Khairiyya and al-Mudmarat, explaining that some are stronger than others: 'al-fatwa' is stronger than 'sahih', 'beh yufata' is stronger than 'al-fatwa 'alayhi', 'asahh' is stronger than 'sahih', and 'ahtat' is stronger than 'ihtiyat'.

The text then addresses the specific question: Is 'sahih' stronger or 'asahh'? It presents two views. The first, from Sharh al-Munya, argues that 'sahih' is stronger because its opposite is 'fasid' (corrupt), while the opposite of 'asahh' is 'sahih'. Hence, if one scholar says 'sahih' and another says 'asahh', the latter agrees with the former that it is sahih, but the former rejects the other opinion as corrupt. So accepting what both agree is sahih is safer. The second view, attributed to the majority (jumhur) as mentioned by Ibn Abd al-Razzaq, is that 'asahh' is stronger. The author of Sharh al-Bayri, quoting al-Bazdawi, explains that 'sahih' implies other views are not sahih, whereas 'asahh' implies other views are sahih (but less strong). However, he cautions that this applies typically; sometimes the opposite of 'asahh' is an anomalous (shadhdha) narration, as in Sharh al-Majma'.

Finally, the passage cites al-Durr al-Mukhtar quoting Risala Adab al-Muftiyyin: If a ruling in a reliable book is annotated with 'asahh', 'awla', or 'arfaq', the mufti has a choice to follow it or its opposite. But if annotated with 'sahih', 'al-ma'khudh bihi', 'beh yufata', or 'alayhi al-fatwa', he cannot contradict it, unless there is another equally authoritative work where the opposite is also marked 'sahih' (e.g., in al-Hidaya one view is 'sahih' and in al-Kafi the opposite is 'sahih'), then the mufti may choose the stronger, more suitable, or more beneficial opinion. This rule is to be memorized.

The import of this passage is that while the hierarchy of terms can be debated, the practical rule for muftis is: 'sahih' and its equivalents are binding, whereas 'asahh' and softer terms allow discretion. This is a key rasm al-mufti principle.

Key Terms
الصحيح (al-sahih): A term indicating a ruling is correct; its opposite is considered corrupt (fasid). In fatwa hierarchy, it is more binding than 'asahh'.
الأصح (al-asahh): A term indicating a ruling is more correct than another, but the other may still be valid (sahih). It is considered weaker than 'sahih' in binding force.
عليه الفتوى (alayhi al-fatwa): A phrase meaning 'the fatwa is upon it', indicating the ruling to be given as fatwa. It is considered a strong indicator, similar to 'sahih'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a ruling in a reliable book is annotated with the word 'asahh', what is the mufti's option?
A) He must give fatwa with that ruling.
B) He may give fatwa with that ruling or its opposite as he wishes.
C) He must give fatwa with the opposite.
D) He cannot give any fatwa on that issue.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fatwa methodology, 'sahih' and its equivalents bind the mufti, while 'asahh' and softer terms grant discretion.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa
advanced
15 min
Rules for Weighing Conflicting Corrections in Fatwa
Rules for mufti when corrections conflict

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules for weighing conflicting corrections (al-sahih vs al-asahh, fatwa wordings) to determine which ruling a mufti should prefer.

Core Idea
Correctness (sahih) alone does not permit fatwa; fatwa wording (lafz al-fatwa) adds permission to act.

When a mufti encounters multiple corrections from different sources, he must navigate a hierarchy of terms. This lesson unpacks the difference between 'sahih' (correct) and 'asahh' (more correct) and establishes clear rules for preferring one ruling over another. By mastering these rules, you will avoid the common pitfall of treating every sahih ruling as immediately operative.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conflicting corrections
Note the exact wording used for each correction: 'sahih', 'asahh', 'bihi yufata', 'alayhi al-fatwa', 'alayhi amal al-umma', etc. This determines the applicable rule.
Pay attention to whether the wording includes permission for fatwa.
2
Classify each correction's type
Determine if the wording is mere correctness (sahih/asahh) or fatwa wording (lafz al-fatwa). Fatwa wording implies both soundness and authorization.
If the wording indicates exclusivity (e.g., 'alayhi al-fatwa'), it is even stronger.
3
Apply the hierarchy of precedence
Fatwa wordings override mere sahih/asahh. If both have fatwa wording, prefer the one indicating exclusivity. If both are the same type (e.g., both 'sahih'), the mufti has free choice.
The strongest wording is 'alayhi amal al-umma' (consensus).
4
Handle exceptions for same book or different imams
If both corrections are in the same book from one imam, 'asahh' automatically overrides 'sahih' (unless a third fasid opinion exists). If from different books, the earlier ikhtilaf about 'sahih' vs 'asahh' applies.
These exceptions preserve the coherence of a single author's work.
5
Select the operative ruling
After applying the rules, choose the ruling that is both correct and authorized for fatwa. If choice remains, prefer the strongest, most suitable, and most beneficial ruling.
Consider changing times and necessity when choosing among sahih options.

هل الصحيح أكد أم الأصح؟ مقابله الفاسد، والأصح مقابله الصحيح، فقد وافق من قال: الأصح قائل الصحيح على أنه صحيح وأما من قال: "الصحيح" فعنده ذلك الأخذ من الحكم الآخر فاسد، فالأخذ بما اتفقا على أنه صحيح أولى بما عند أحدهما فاسد. هو وذكر العلامة ابن عبد الرزاق في شرحه على "الدر المختار": أن المشهور عند الجمهور أن الأصح أكد من الصحيح. وفي شرح البيري: قال في "الطراز المذهب" ناقلا عن حاشية البزدوي: قوله: "هو الصحيح" يقتضي أن يكون غيره غير صحيح، ولفظ الأصح يقتضي أن يكون غيره صحيحا أقول: ينبغي أن يقيد ذلك بالغالب؛ لأنا وجدنا مقابل "الأصح" الرواية الشاذة، كما في شرح المجمع. وفي "الدر المختار" بعد نقله حاصل ما مر : " ثم رأيت في "رسالة آداب المفتين : إذا ذيلت رواية في كتاب معتمد رواية في كتاب معتمد بالأصح، أو الأولى، أو الأرفق، ونحوها، فله أن يفتي بها وبمخالفتها أيضا أيا شاء. وإذا ذيلت بالصحيح، أو المأخوذ به، أو به يفتى أو عليه الفتوى لم يفت مخالفها، إلا إذا كان في "الهداية" مثلا هو الصحيح، وفي "الكافي" بمخالفه هو الصحيح، فيخيّر، فيختار الأقوى عنده، والأليق، والأصلح. فليحفظ.

قلت: وحاصل هذا كله

[ضوابط التصحيح]

ضوابط التصحيح

١- أنه إذا صحح كل من الروايتين بلفظ واحد كأن ذكر في كل واحدة منهما هو الصحيح، أو الأصح، أو به يفتى - تخير المفتي. ۲ - وإذا اختلف اللفظ، فإن كان أحدهما لفظ الفتوى، فهو أولى؛ لأنه لا يفتى إلا بما هو صحيح، وليس كل صحيح يفتى به؛ لأن الصحيح في نفسه قد لا يفتى به؛ لكون غيره أوفق لتغير الزمان، وللضرورة، ونحو ذلك. فما فيه لفظ الفتوى يتضمن شيئين: أحدهما: الإذن بالفتوى به، والآخر صحته؛ لأن الإفتاء به تصحيح له، بخلاف ما فيه لفظ الصحيح، أو الأصح مثلا. وإن كان لفظ الفتوى في كل منهما فإن كان أحدهما يفيد الحصر، مثل به يفتى أو عليه الفتوى، فهو الأولى. ومثله بل أولى لفظ "عليه عمل "الأمة" ؛ لأنه يفيد الإجماع. ٤ - وإن لم يكن لفظ الفتوى في واحد منهما فإن كان أحدهما بلفظ الأصح؛ والآخر بلفظ الصحيح، فعلى الخلاف السابق. لكن هذا فيما إذا كان التصحيحان في كتابين، أما لو كانا في كتاب واحد من إمام واحد، فلا يتأتى الخلاف في تقديم الأصح على الصحيح؛ لأن إشعار الصحيح بأن مقابله فاسد لا يتأتى فيه بعد التصريح بأن مقابله أصح، إلا إذا كان في المسألة قول ثالث يكون هو الفاسد وكذا لو ذكر تصحيحين عن إمامين ثم قال: إن هذا التصحيح
Is 'sahih' stronger or 'asahh'? Its opposite is fasid, and 'asahh' its opposite is sahih. So he who said 'asahh' agreed with the one who said 'sahih' that it is sahih. But as for he who said 'sahih', in his view that taking from the other ruling is fasid. So taking what they both agreed is sahih is more appropriate than what in one of them is fasid. It is. And the scholar Ibn Abd al-Razzaq mentioned in his commentary on al-Durr al-Mukhtar that the well-known position among the majority is that 'asahh' is stronger than 'sahih'. And in Sharh al-Bayri: He said in al-Tiraz al-Madhhab, quoting from Hashiyat al-Bazdawi: His saying 'it is the sahih' necessitates that other than it is not sahih, and the word 'asahh' necessitates that other than it is sahih. I say: it should be restricted to the general case, because we find the opposite of 'asahh' to be a rare transmission, as in Sharh al-Majma. And in al-Durr al-Mukhtar after quoting the gist of what preceded: 'Then I saw in Risala Adab al-Muftin: If a narration in a reliable book is annotated with 'asahh', or 'awla', or 'arfaq', and the like, he may give fatwa according to it and also according to its contrary, whichever he wishes. And if it is annotated with 'sahih', or 'al-ma'khudh bihi', or 'bihi yufata', or 'alayhi al-fatwa', he may not give fatwa contrary to it, except if in al-Hidayah for example it is 'al-sahih', and in al-Kafi its contrary is 'al-sahih', then he chooses, preferring the strongest in his view, the most suitable, and the most beneficial. So let this be memorized. I said: The summary of all this is: [Rules of Correction] Rules of Correction: 1. If each of the two narrations is corrected with the same wording, such that it is mentioned in each of them 'it is the sahih', or 'asahh', or 'bihi yufata', the mufti has a choice. 2. And if the phrasing differs, and one of them is a fatwa wording, then it is given precedence, because fatwa is only given according to what is sahih, and not every sahih can be given fatwa according to it, because the sahih in itself may not be given fatwa due to another being more suitable due to change of time, necessity, and the like. So what has the fatwa wording includes two things: permission to give fatwa by it, and its soundness; since giving fatwa by it constitutes a correction of it, unlike what merely has the wording 'sahih' or 'asahh'. 3. And if the fatwa wording is present in both, and one of them indicates exclusivity, such as 'bihi yufata' or 'alayhi al-fatwa', then it is given precedence. Similarly, and even more so, the wording 'alayhi amal al-umma' because it indicates consensus. 4. And if the fatwa wording is not present in either, and one is with the wording 'asahh' and the other with the wording 'sahih', then it is according to the earlier disagreement. But this is if the two corrections are in two books. However, if they are in one book from one imam, then the disagreement about giving precedence to 'asahh' over 'sahih' does not arise, because the indication of 'sahih' that its opposite is fasid cannot be entertained after the explicit statement that its opposite is asahh, unless there is a third opinion in the issue that is the fasid one. Similarly, if two corrections are mentioned from two imams, then he says: this correction...
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage from Sharh 'Uqud Rasm al-Mufti is a methodological discussion about the hierarchy of terms used in fiqh corrections and the rules a mufti must follow when faced with conflicting corrections from different sources.

The opening question asks whether 'sahih' or 'asahh' is stronger. Some scholars hold that 'asahh' is stronger because it implies that the opposite is also sahih, while 'sahih' implies the opposite is fasid. Others reverse the preference. The author then quotes several authorities: Ibn Abd al-Razzaq states that the majority consider 'asahh' stronger. Al-Bayri quotes al-Bazdawi's distinction: 'sahih' denies validity to any other view, while 'asahh' acknowledges other views as sahih. The author cautions that this applies generally but exceptions exist, such as when the opposite of 'asahh' is a rare (shadhdh) report.

Then the text shifts to rules for a mufti when he encounters different annotations in reliable books. If a narration is annotated with words like 'asahh', 'awla', 'arfaq', the mufti may follow it or its opposite as he chooses. However, if it is annotated with 'sahih', 'al-ma'khudh bihi', 'bihi yufata', or 'alayhi al-fatwa', he cannot oppose it, unless there is a similar correction in another book. In that case, he chooses the strongest, most suitable, and most beneficial ruling.

The author then condenses this into four rules:
1. If both narrations have the same type of correction (e.g., both say 'sahih' or both say 'asahh'), the mufti has a free choice.
2. If the wording differs and one has a fatwa wording (like 'bihi yufata'), that one is given precedence because fatwa implies both soundness and permission to act, whereas 'sahih' only indicates soundness which may still be unsuitable for fatwa due to changed circumstances.
3. If both have fatwa wording but one indicates exclusivity (like 'alayhi al-fatwa'), that one is given precedence. Even more so if it says 'alayhi amal al-umma', as it suggests consensus.
4. If neither has fatwa wording and one is 'asahh' while the other is 'sahih', the earlier difference of opinion applies. However, this only applies when the corrections are in different books. If both corrections are from the same imam in one book, 'asahh' automatically takes precedence over 'sahih' because the imam's explicit preference overrides the implication of 'sahih'. An exception arises if there is a third opinion that is clearly fasid.

The passage emphasizes that the mufti must carefully weigh the authority and wording of corrections, understanding that not every sahih ruling is suitable for fatwa due to practical considerations like time, custom, and necessity.

Key Terms
الصحيح (al-sahih): A ruling that is correct in itself, but its opposite is considered fasid (invalid) in the view of the one who says it.
الأصح (al-asahh): A stronger (more correct) ruling among multiple sahih options; its opposite is also sahih.
لفظ الفتوى (lafz al-fatwa): Phrase like 'به يفتى' (by it fatwa is given) or 'عليه الفتوى' (upon it is fatwa) indicating that this ruling is the operative one for fatwa.
به يفتى (bihi yufata): A phrase used to indicate that the ruling is the one to be given in fatwa, implying both soundness and current applicability.
عليه عمل الأمة (alayhi amal al-umma): A phrase indicating that the ruling is the established practice of the Muslim community, implying consensus.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If one source says 'هو الصحيح' and another source says 'به يفتى', which ruling should the mufti prefer according to the passage?
A) The one with 'هو الصحيح'
B) The one with 'به يفتى'
C) Neither; the mufti may choose freely
D) It depends on the madhhab of the author

Key Takeaway
The mufti must not only know which ruling is sahih but also whether it is authorized for fatwa (fatwa wording), and when corrections conflict, he follows a hierarchy of wordings and chooses the most suitable.

Preferring between Two Corrected Rulings
Rules for preferring one corrected ruling over another when both are corrected.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules for preferring one corrected ruling over another when both are corrected, by identifying the rank of correcting scholars and the wording used.

Core Idea
When two rulings are corrected, prefer the correction of the more knowledgeable scholar and the wording 'asahh' over 'sahih'.

In Hanafi fatwa methodology, a mufti often encounters multiple corrected rulings. This lesson provides the criteria for preferring one over another, based on the scholar's rank and the explicit wording. Understanding these rules ensures that the mufti issues fatwas based on the strongest authority.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two corrected rulings and their correcting scholars
Locate the two rulings in question and note which scholar corrected each and what wording (e.g., 'asahh', 'sahih', 'ahwat') was used.
These scholars are often from later Hanafi works like al-Khaniya or al-Bazzaziyya.
2
Determine the relative knowledge of the correcting scholars
Compare the scholarly rank: if one is more knowledgeable, his correction is preferred regardless of wording.
Qadi Khan is given as an example of a highly authoritative scholar.
3
If scholars are of equal rank, examine the wording
If both scholars are equal in knowledge, then prefer the ruling that uses 'asahh' over 'sahih', or 'asahh' over other terms like 'ahwat'.
If only one ruling is explicitly corrected with 'asahh' and the other is silent, taking the 'asahh' is preferable but not obligatory.
4
Apply the preference rule
Combine the two factors: first prioritize by scholar knowledge; if equal, prioritize by wording strength.
The mufti may choose freely if both scholars equal and wording identical.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني أصح من الأول مثلا، فإنه لا شك أن مراده ترجيح ما بكونه أصح، ويقع ذلك كثرا في تصحيح العلامة قاسم. وإن كان كل منهما بلفظ الأصح أو الصحيح، فلا شبهة في أنه يتخيّر بينهما إذا كان الإمامان المصححان في رتبة واحدة. ٦- أما لو كان أحدهما أعلم فإنه يختار تصحيحه، كما لو كان أحدهما في "الخانية" والآخر في "البزازية" مثلا، فإن تصحيح قاضي خان أقوى، فقد قال العلامة قاسم: "إن قاضي خان من أحق من يعتمد على تصحيحه". وكذا يتخيّر إذا صرح بتصحيح إحداهما فقط بلفظ الأصح، أو الأحوط، أو الأولى، أو الأرفق، وسكت عن تصحيح الأخرى، فإن هذا اللفظ يفيد صحة الأخرى، لكن الأولى الأخذ بما الأصح؛ لزيادة صحتها. وكذا لو صرح في إحداهما بالأصح، وفي الأخرى بالصحيح، فإن الأولى الأخذ بالأصح.
The second is more correct than the first, for example. There is no doubt that his intent is to prefer what is more correct, and this occurs frequently in the correction of 'Allama Qasim. If both are expressed with the word 'asahh' or 'sahih', then there is no doubt that he chooses between them if the two correcting imams are of the same rank. 6- But if one of them is more knowledgeable, then he chooses his correction, as if one is in al-Khaniya and the other in al-Bazzaziyya, for example, then the correction of Qadi Khan is stronger, for 'Allama Qasim said: 'Qadi Khan is among those most entitled to rely on his correction.' And likewise he chooses if he explicitly corrected only one with the word 'asahh', 'ahwat', 'awla', or 'arfaq', and remained silent about correcting the other, for this word indicates the validity of the other, but it is preferable to take the 'asahh' due to its greater authenticity. And likewise if he explicitly said 'asahh' in one and 'sahih' in the other, then it is preferable to take the 'asahh'.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage discusses the internal Hanafi rules for preferring one corrected ruling over another when there is apparent conflict. It is a rasm al-mufti rule. The author clarifies that when a scholar says 'the second is more correct than the first', his intent is to prefer the more correct one, not to reject the first entirely. If both rulings are called 'asahh' or 'sahih', the mufti may choose between them if the two correcting scholars are of equal rank. However, if one scholar is more knowledgeable, his correction is preferred — e.g., Qadi Khan's correction in al-Khaniya is stronger than that in al-Bazzaziyya. Additionally, if only one correction is explicitly labeled 'asahh', 'ahwat', etc., and the other is not corrected, the mufti may still choose either, but taking the 'asahh' is preferable. Similarly, if one is 'asahh' and the other 'sahih', the 'asahh' is preferred.

Key Terms
الأصح (al-asahh): The more correct of two rulings in the madhhab, indicating a higher degree of authenticity.
الصحيح (al-sahih): A correct narration or ruling in the madhhab, but may be less strong than 'asahh'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The one in al-Bazzaziyya
B) The one in al-Khaniya
C) Both are equal
D) It depends on the mufti's preference

Key Takeaway
Prefer the correction of the more knowledgeable scholar; if equal, prefer the stricter wording 'asahh' over 'sahih'.

Tarjih Rules When Corrections Conflict
Poem listing tarjih rules with commentary

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the preponderating factors that limit a mufti's choice when two corrected statements conflict and apply them to a scenario.

Core Idea
The mufti's choice is not absolute; specific factors like 'asahh', fatwa label, or presence in mutun preponderate.

When a mufti encounters two corrected rulings on a single issue, the default rule gives him a choice. However, this freedom is limited by explicit preponderating factors that guide which ruling must be taken. The poem below, from a classical Hanafi text, memorably lists these factors, and the commentary explains the key ones. This lesson unpacks the rule so you can correctly prioritize among conflicting corrections.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
The poem begins by stating that if two corrected statements exist, the mufti may choose either. This is the baseline position.
Remember: this is only when no preponderating factor exists.
2
Recognize the exception
The word 'except' (إلا) introduces the limiting factors. Only when one of these factors is present does the choice become restricted.
The factors are listed in the remaining lines of the poem.
3
Memorize the list of factors
The poem enumerates several factors: 'asahh' vs 'sahih', fatwa-endorsed, in mutun, statement of Imam Abu Hanifa, apparent meaning of narrated, consensus of major scholars, istihsan, benefit for endowments, appropriateness for time, clarity of proof.
Focus on the first three from the commentary as they are most commonly encountered.
4
Apply the hierarchy
If a factor is present, the mufti must take the preponderated statement. If multiple factors point to different statements, deeper analysis is needed (beyond this lesson).
For now, assume each factor independently gives priority.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنْ تجد تصحيح قولين وَرَد فاختر لما شئت فكل معتى إلا إذ كانا صحيحا وأصح أو قيل: ذا يفتي به فقد رُجح أو كان في المتون أو قول الإمام أو ظاهر المروي أو جُلّ العظام قال به، أو كان الاستحسانا أو زاد للأوقاف نفعا بانا أو كان ذا أوفق للزمان أو كان ذا أوضح في البرهان هذا إذا تعارض التصحيحُ أو لم يكن أصلا به تصريح فتأخذ الذي له مرجع مما علمته فهذا الأوضح

قواعد الترجيح عند تعارض التصحيح]
لما ذكرت علامات التصحيح لقول من الأقوال، وأن بعض ألفاظ التصحيح أكد من بعض، وهذا إنما تظهر ثمرته عند التعارض بأن كان التصحيح لقولين فصلت ذلك تفصيلا حسنا لم أسبق إليه، أخذا مما مهدته قبل هذا وذلك أن قولهم: "إذا كان في المسألة قولان مصححان، فالمفتي بالخيار" ليس على إطلاقه، بل ذاك إذا لم يكن لأحدهما مرجح قبل التصحيح أو بعده. الأول من المرجحات ما إذا كان تصحيح أحدهما بلفظ الصحيح والآخر بلفظ الأصح وتقدم الكلام فيه، وأن المشهور ترجيح الأصح على الصحيح. الثاني : ما إذا كان أحدهما بلفظ الفتوى والآخر بغيره، كما تقدم بيانه. الثالث: ما إذا كان أحد القولين المصححين في المتون، والآخر في غيرها؛ لأنه عند عدم التصحيح لأحد القولين يقدم ما في المتون؛ لأنها
And if you find a correction of two statements that have come, choose whichever you wish, for each is considered valid. Except when both are [called] sahih and asahh, or it is said: 'This is given fatwa with' and it is preponderated, or it is in the mutun, or it is the statement of the Imam, or the apparent of the narrated, or most of the great scholars said it, or it is istihsan, or it adds manifest benefit for endowments, or it is more appropriate for the time, or it is clearer in proof. This is when there is conflict in correction, or there is originally no explicit correction, then take that which has a reference from what you know, for this is clearer.

Rules of preference when correction conflicts:
Since I mentioned the signs of correction for a statement among statements, and that some words of correction are more emphatic than others, and this manifests its fruit only at conflict, when there are two corrected statements. I have detailed that in a good detail not preceded by me, taking from what I laid before this: that their statement: 'If there are two corrected statements in a matter, the mufti has a choice' is not absolute; rather, that is when there is no preponderating factor for one before correction or after. The first of the preponderating factors: when the correction of one is with the word 'sahih' and the other with 'asahh', and the discussion has preceded, and that the well-known is preferring 'asahh' over 'sahih'. The second: when one is with the word of fatwa and the other without, as its explanation has preceded. The third: when one of the two corrected statements is in the mutun, and the other outside them; because when there is no correction of either statement, what is in the mutun is preferred, because they are...
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage begins with a poem (nazm) summarizing the rules of tarjih when there are conflicting corrections. The poem states that if two corrected statements exist, the mufti may choose unless specific preponderating factors exist: one is 'asahh' and the other 'sahih', one is labeled as the position for fatwa, one is in the mutun (core texts), one is the statement of Imam Abu Hanifa, one is the apparent meaning of the narrated, one is supported by most major scholars, one is based on istihsan, one brings more benefit for endowments (awqaf), or one is more appropriate for the time or clearer in proof. The commentary explains that the statement 'the mufti has a choice' is not absolute; it applies only if there is no preponderating factor before or after correction. It then lists three specific factors: (1) 'asahh' over 'sahih', (2) fatwa-endorsed over not, (3) in mutun over others. The commentary is based on the author's earlier work.

Key Terms
المتون (al-mutun): The core authoritative texts of the Hanafi madhhab, such as Mukhtasar al-Quduri, Kanz al-Daqa'iq, etc.
الفتوى (al-fatwa): A ruling explicitly designated as the position for giving fatwa, often indicated by 'بِهِ يُفْتَى'.
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Juristic preference based on considerations of public interest, equity, or ease, often overriding qiyas.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) One is from a later fatwa collection
D) One is more appropriate for the time

Key Takeaway
When two corrected statements conflict, the mufti's choice is limited by specific preponderating factors; the strongest are 'asahh', fatwa label, and presence in mutun.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa

Nine Preference Rules When Authenticated Rulings Conflict
Enumerates nine criteria for preferring one authenticated ruling over another.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the nine Hanafi criteria for preferring one authenticated ruling over another when tas'hihs conflict.

Core Idea
When two authenticated Hanafi rulings conflict, use nine hierarchical criteria to prefer one – not free choice.

When a mufti faces two equally authenticated positions (tas'hih), the common maxim 'the mufti may choose' is not absolute. The later Hanafi masters laid down nine specific preference criteria that must be applied in order. This lesson unpacks each of those nine rules, grounding the mufti's decision in a structured methodology rather than personal whim.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the default rule and its limitation
Understand that the mufti normally chooses when two positions are equally authenticated, but this is only when no preference exists before or after tas'hih.
The maxim 'if two are authenticated, the mufti has a choice' is qualified.
2
Check authentication terms and explicit fatwa designation
First, prefer 'asahh' over 'sahih'; then, prefer a ruling explicitly labeled 'fatwa' over one without.
These are the first two criteria.
3
Assess textual source and transmission strength
Prefer positions found in the primary texts (mutun) over later works; prefer the saying of Imam Abu Hanifa over his companions; prefer zahir al-riwaya over other transmissions.
Criteria three to five.
4
Consider scholarly consensus and legal methodology
Prefer the majority opinion of the great shaykhs; and generally prefer istihsan over qiyas.
Criteria six and seven.
5
Apply case-specific and contextual criteria
In waqf matters, prefer the more beneficial ruling; in all cases, prefer the ruling more in harmony with custom and easier for people.
Criteria eight and nine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن تجد تصحيح قولين ورد فاختر لما شئت فكل معتمد إلا إذ كانا صحيحا وأصح أو قيل: ذا يفتي به فقد رجح أو كان في المتون أو قول الإمام أو ظاهر المروي أو جل العظام قال به، أو كان الاستحسانا أو زاد للأوقاف نفعا بانا أو كان ذا أوفق للزمان أو كان ذا أوضح في البرهان هذا إذا تعارض التصحيحُ أو لم يكن أصلا به تصريح فتأخذ الذي له مرجع مما علمته فهذا الأوضح
قواعد الترجيح عند تعارض التصحيح
لما ذكرت علامات التصحيح لقول من الأقوال، وأن بعض ألفاظ التصحيح أكد من بعض، وهذا إنما تظهر ثمرته عند التعارض بأن كان التصحيح لقولين فصلت ذلك تفصيلا حسنا لم أسبق إليه، أخذا مما مهدته قبل هذا وذلك أن قولهم: "إذا كان في المسألة قولان مصححان، فالمفتي بالخيار" ليس على إطلاقه، بل ذاك إذا لم يكن لأحدهما مرجح قبل التصحيح أو بعده. الأول من المرجحات ما إذا كان تصحيح أحدهما بلفظ الصحيح والآخر بلفظ الأصح وتقدم الكلام فيه، وأن المشهور ترجيح الأصح على الصحيح. الثاني: ما إذا كان أحدهما بلفظ الفتوى والآخر بغيره، كما تقدم بيانه. الثالث: ما إذا كان أحد القولين المصححين في المتون، والآخر في غيرها؛ لأنه عند عدم التصحيح لأحد القولين يقدم ما في المتون؛ لأنها الموضوعة لنقل المذهب كما مر فكذا إذا تعارض التصحيحان، ولذا قال في "البحر" في باب قضاء الفوائت: فقد اختلف التصحيح والفتوى والعمل بما وافق المتون أولى. الرابع: ما إذا كان أحدهما قول الإمام الأعظم، والآخر قول بعض أصحابه؛ لأنه عند عدم الترجيح لأحدهما يقدم قول الإمام، كما مر بيانه، فكذا بعده. الخامس: ما إذا كان أحدهما ظاهر الرواية، فيقدم على الآخر. قال في "البحر" من كتاب الرضاع: الفتوى إذا اختلفت كان الترجيح لظاهر الرواية. وفيه من باب المصرف: إذا اختلف التصحيح وجب الفحص عن ظاهر الرواية والرجوع إليه. السادس: ما إذا كان أحد القولين المصححين قال به جل المشايخ العظام. ففي شرح البيري على الأشباه: أن المقرر عن المشايخ متى اختلف في المسألة، فالعبرة بما قاله الأكثر. وقدمنا نحوه عن "الحاوي القدسي". السابع: ما إذا كان أحدهما الاستحسان والآخر القياس؛ لما قدمناه أن الأرجح الاستحسان إلا في مسائل. الثامن: ما إذا كان أحدهما أنفع للوقف؛ لما صرحوا به في "الحاوي القدسي" وغيره: "من أنه يفتى بما هو أنفع للوقف فيما اختلف العلماء فيه". التاسع: ما إذا كان أحدهما أوفق لأهل الزمان؛ فإن ما كان أوفق لعرفهم أو أسهل عليهم، فهو أولى بالاعتماد عليه. ولذا أفتوا بقول
If you find authentication (tas'hih) of two statements, then choose whichever you wish, for each is reliable. Except when they are 'sahih' and 'asahh', or it is said: 'this is the one given fatwa' then it is given preference. Or if it is in the primary texts (mutun), or the saying of the Imam, or the manifest narration (zahir al-riwaya), or the majority of great scholars said it. Or if it is istihsan, or it gives clear benefit to endowments, or it is more in harmony with the times, or it is clearer in proof. This is when authentication conflicts, or there is originally no explicit statement; then adopt what has a reference, from what you know—this is the clearer. Rules of preference when authentication conflicts: When I mentioned the signs of authentication for a saying among sayings, and that some expressions of authentication are stronger than others—the benefit of this appears when there is conflict, where authentication applies to two statements. I detail this in a good detail not preceded by anyone, based on what I laid down before. That is, their saying: 'If there are two authenticated statements in a matter, the mufti has a choice' is not absolute, but only when there is no preference for one before or after authentication. The first of the preferences: when one is authenticated with the term 'sahih' and the other with 'asahh', and the discussion preceded, and the well-known position is preferring 'asahh' over 'sahih'. Second: when one is with the term 'fatwa' and the other without it, as its explanation preceded. Third: when one of the two authenticated statements is in the primary texts (mutun) and the other outside them; because when there is no authentication for either statement, that in the mutun is given precedence, since they are established for transmitting the madhhab, as passed; similarly when authentications conflict. Thus it is stated in 'al-Bahr' in the chapter of making up missed prayers: 'When authentication and fatwa differ, acting upon what agrees with the mutun is better.' Fourth: when one is the saying of the great Imam (Abu Hanifa) and the other of some of his companions; because when there is no preference for one, the saying of the Imam is given precedence, as its explanation preceded—so likewise after authentication. Fifth: when one is 'zahir al-riwaya' (manifest narration), it is given preference over the other. It is stated in 'al-Bahr' from the book of rada' (fosterage): 'When fatwa differs, preference is given to zahir al-riwaya.' And in it from the chapter of the channel: 'When authentication differs, investigation of zahir al-riwaya is obligatory and recourse to it.' Sixth: when one of the two authenticated statements is the saying of the majority of the great shaykhs. In Sharh al-Bayri on al-Ashbah: 'The established position regarding the shaykhs is that whenever there is disagreement in a matter, the criterion is what the majority said.' And we presented something similar from 'al-Hawi al-Qudsi'. Seventh: when one is istihsan and the other qiyas; because we have presented that istihsan is preferable except in certain matters. Eighth: when one is more beneficial for the endowment; because they explicitly state in 'al-Hawi al-Qudsi' and others: 'Fatwa is given according to what is more beneficial for the endowment when scholars differ.' Ninth: when one is more in harmony with the people of the time; for what is more in harmony with their custom or easier for them is more deserving of reliance. And thus they gave fatwa with the saying
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of the rules of preference (tarjih) when two or more rulings have been authenticated (tas'hih) within the Hanafi school. The author begins with a poetic summary (nazm) encapsulating the nine criteria, then explains each. The key point: the blanket statement 'if two sayings are authenticated, the mufti may choose' is not absolute; preference must be determined by specific hierarchical rules. The nine criteria are: (1) 'asahh' over 'sahih'—when one is tagged 'sahih' (authentic) and the other 'asahh' (more authentic), the latter is preferred. (2) If one is explicitly said to be 'the position of fatwa', that is preferred over one without such designation. (3) A statement found in the primary works (mutun) of the madhhab is preferred over one in later commentaries. The author cites al-Bahr al-Ra'iq supporting this. (4) The saying of Imam Abu Hanifa (al-Imam al-A'zam) is preferred over that of his students (Muhammad, Abu Yusuf) in cases of conflict. (5) A ruling transmitted as 'zahir al-riwaya' (i.e., from the six authoritative works of Muhammad al-Shaybani) is given precedence over non-zahir al-riwaya. The author references al-Bahr. (6) When a ruling is adopted by the majority of the great shaykhs (early Hanafi jurists), it is preferred. This is based on the principle that the majority opinion carries weight. (7) Istihsan (juristic preference) is preferred over qiyas (analogy) in general, except for certain exceptions. (8) In matters of waqf, the opinion that is more beneficial for the endowment is chosen, as per the explicit fatwa in al-Hawi al-Qudsi. (9) The opinion that is more in harmony with the custom (urf) of the time and easier for people is preferred. The author notes that this has been acted upon. The passage is taken from Sharh Uqud Rasm al-Mufti by Nizam al-Din al-Balhi, and these rules guide the mufti in issuing fatwas when multiple authenticated positions exist.

Key Terms
تصحيح (tas'hih): The act of identifying and declaring a particular ruling as the correct/authentic relied-upon position within the Hanafi school, often indicated by phrases like 'sahih' or 'asahh'.
الصحيح (al-sahih): A term used to authenticate a position, meaning it is the correct (sahih) opinion according to later authorities.
الأصح (al-asahh): A term meaning 'more authentic' or 'most authentic', used to rank one authenticated position higher than another that is merely 'sahih'.
المتون (al-mutun): The primary, authoritative texts of the Hanafi school, such as al-Hidayah, al-Mukhtar, al-Kanz, etc., which are the core references for transmitting the madhhab.
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwaya): The manifest/well-known transmission from the three founders (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) through Imam Muhammad's six authoritative works (al-Mabsut, etc.).
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Juristic preference, a method of departing from qiyas in favor of a more suitable ruling due to custom, necessity, or equity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when should the mufti have free choice between two authenticated statements?
A) Always
B) Only when one is in the mutun
C) Only when there is no preference before or after authentication
D) Only when the Imam's saying is unknown

Key Takeaway
The mufti must apply nine hierarchical criteria when two authenticated rulings conflict; free choice is a fallback only when no criterion applies.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa
intermediate

Ten Rules for Preference When Tashih Conflicts
Listing ten rules for preferring one tashih over another.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ten preference rules for resolving conflicts between corrected opinions (tashih) in the Hanafi school, and apply them to prioritize one opinion over another in fatwa.

Core Idea
When two opinions are both 'corrected', the mufti applies ten rules—from authorial rank to custom change—to choose the stronger view.

The Hanafi school is vast, and later mujtahids often issue tashih (corrections) identifying the preferred position. But what happens when two tashihs conflict? In this passage, the author lists ten rules (from the fourth to the tenth) that guide a mufti in choosing between them. These rules ensure consistency and respect the school's hierarchy—from the authority of Imam Abu Hanifa to the strength of evidence, and even the changing needs of the time. Mastering these rules is essential for a mufti who must navigate apparent contradictions within the madhhab.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conflict
Recognize when two opinions are both said to be 'corrected' (musahhah) and no explicit tarjih is given. The rules apply only when each opinion is equally valid in terms of correctness.
The passage states: 'All this is when tashih conflicts because each of the two statements is equal in correctness.'
2
Apply the hierarchical rules in order
Go through the ten rules (4–10) one by one, checking which applies to the conflicting opinions. The rules are not strictly sequential but provide multiple angles: authorial rank (rule 4), transmission strength (rule 5), scholarly consensus (rule 6), istihsan vs qiyas (rule 7), waqf benefit (rule 8), harmony with the times (rule 9), and strength of evidence (rule 10).
The text presents them as independent factors; the mufti should consider all that are relevant.
3
Consider the particular context
If the case involves a waqf, rule 8 (benefit for the waqf) takes special consideration. If the times have changed, rule 9 (harmony with custom/necessity) may override other factors. For example, the change from requiring apparent integrity to formal tazkiyah was due to changing times.
Rule 9 is illustrated with historical examples: tazkiyah of witnesses and hiring for teaching.
4
Prioritize by strength of evidence
Rule 10 reminds that the ultimate criterion is the strength of the proof (dalil). If one opinion has a clearer or stronger textual basis, it should be preferred, even if other rules point differently. This rule acts as a safety net.
The passage says: 'preference is by strength of proof.'
5
Formulate the fatwa
Once the preferred opinion is identified using these rules, state it as the mufta bihi (operative ruling). Justify the choice by referencing the applicable rule(s). This ensures transparency and methodological rigor.
If no explicit tashih is given for either opinion, apply the same preference factors (e.g., being in the mutun, or from the Imam, or Zahir al-Riwaya).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع: ما إذا كان أحدهما قول الإمام الأعظم، والآخر قول بعض أصحابه؛ لأنه عند عدم الترجيح لأحدهما يقدم قول الإمام، كما مر بيانه، فكذا بعده.
الخامس: ما إذا كان أحدهما ظاهر الرواية، فيقدم على الآخر. قال في "البحر" من كتاب الرضاع: الفتوى إذا اختلفت كان الترجيح لظاهر الرواية. وفيه من باب المصرف: إذا اختلف التصحيح وجب الفحص عن ظاهر الرواية والرجوع إليه.
السادس: ما إذا كان أحد القولين المصححين قال به جلّ المشايخ العظام. ففي شرح البيري على الأشباه: أن المقرر عن المشايخ متى اختلف في المسألة، فالعبرة بما قاله الأكثر. وقدمنا نحوه عن "الحاوي القدسي".
السابع: ما إذا كان أحدهما الاستحسان والآخر القياس؛ لما قدمناه أن الأرجح الاستحسان إلا في مسائل.
الثامن: ما إذا كان أحدهما أنفع للوقف؛ لما صرحوا به في "الحاوي القدسي" وغيره: "من أنه يفتى بما هو أنفع للوقف فيما اختلف العلماء فيه".
التاسع: ما إذا كان أحدهما أوفق لأهل الزمان؛ فإن ما كان أوفق لعرفهم أو أسهل عليهم، فهو أولى بالاعتماد عليه. ولذا أفتوا بقول الإمامين في مسألة تزكية الشهود وعدم القضاء بظاهر العدالة؛ لتغير أحوال الزمان؛ فإن الإمام كان في القرن الذي شهد له رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم بالخيرية بخلاف عصر هما؛ فإنه قد فشى فيه الكذب، فلا بد فيه من التزكية وكذا عدلوا عن قول أئمتنا الثلاثة في عدم جواز الاستئجار على التعليم ونحوه؛ لتغير الزمان ووجود الضرورة، إلى القول بجوازه، كما مر بيانه.
العاشر: ما إذا كان أحدهما دليله أوضح وأظهر، كما تقدم أن الترجيح بقوة الدليل. فحيث وجد تصحيحان، ورأى من كان له أهلية النظر في الدليل أن دليل أحدهما أقوى، فالعمل به أولى. هذا كله إذا تعارض التصحيح؛ لأن كل واحد من القولين مساو للآخر في الصحة فإذا كان في أحدهما زيادة قوة من جهة أخرى، يكون العمل به أولى من العمل بالآخر. وكذا إذا لم يصرح بتصحيح واحد من القولين فيقدم ما فيه مرجح من هذه المرجحات، ككونه في المتون، أو قول الإمام، أو ظاهر الرواية. إلخ.
Fourth: When one of them is the statement of the Greatest Imam (Abu Hanifa) and the other is the statement of some of his companions; because when there is no preference for one over the other, the Imam's statement is given precedence, as its explanation has passed, so likewise after him. Fifth: When one of them is from Zahir al-Riwaya, it is given precedence over the other. It is said in al-Bahr from the book of al-Rada': If a fatwa differs, preference is given to Zahir al-Riwaya. And in it from the chapter of al-Musraf: If tashih differs, it is necessary to investigate Zahir al-Riwaya and refer to it. Sixth: When one of the two corrected statements is held by the majority of the great mashayikh. In Sharh al-Bayri on al-Ashbah: It is established from the mashayikh that whenever a matter differs, consideration is given to what the majority said. We have presented similar from al-Hawi al-Qudsi. Seventh: When one of them is istihsan and the other is qiyas; because we have presented that istihsan is preferable except in certain issues. Eighth: When one of them is more beneficial for the waqf; because they have stated in al-Hawi al-Qudsi and elsewhere: "A fatwa is given according to what is more beneficial for the waqf when scholars differ." Ninth: When one of them is more in harmony with the people of the time; for what is more in harmony with their custom or easier for them is more deserving of reliance. Thus they gave fatwa with the statement of the two Imams in the matter of tazkiyah of witnesses and not ruling by apparent integrity due to changing times; for the Imam was in a century that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) attested to goodness, unlike their era where falsehood had spread, so tazkiyah is necessary. Similarly, they departed from the statement of our three imams regarding the impermissibility of hiring for teaching and similar matters due to changing times and necessity, to the statement permitting it, as its explanation has passed. Tenth: When one of them has a clearer and more apparent proof; as has been presented that preference is by strength of proof. Wherever two tashihs are found, and a person qualified to examine the proof sees that the proof of one is stronger, acting upon it is more worthy. All this is when tashih conflicts because each of the two statements is equal in correctness; if there is additional strength in one from another perspective, acting upon it is more worthy than acting upon the other. Similarly, if no tashih of either statement is explicitly mentioned, then that which has a preference factor among these preferentials is given precedence, such as being in the mutun, or the Imam's statement, or Zahir al-Riwaya, etc.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage lists ten rules (from the fourth to the tenth) for a mufti to choose between two equally corrected (musahhah) opinions in the Hanafi school. The rules are hierarchical and aim to resolve apparent contradictions. The fourth rule states that if one opinion is from Imam Abu Hanifa and the other from his students (like Abu Yusuf or Muhammad), the Imam's view is preferred unless there is a specific reason to choose otherwise. The fifth rule gives priority to a ruling that is part of Zahir al-Riwaya—the six core works of Imam Muhammad—over those from Nawadir. The sixth rule looks to the majority of senior scholars (mashayikh) for support. The seventh prefers istihsan (juristic preference) over qiyas (analogy) as a general principle, with some exceptions. The eighth rule, specific to endowments (awqaf), instructs the mufti to choose the opinion most beneficial for the waqf. The ninth rule considers the changing customs and needs of the time, illustrated by the shift from requiring apparent integrity (zahir al-adala) to requiring formal tazkiyah of witnesses due to increased dishonesty, and the permission to hire for teaching due to necessity. The tenth rule returns to the strength of evidence (dalil) as the ultimate criterion. These rules ensure that the mufti acts with methodological consistency, not personal preference.

Key Terms
التصحيح (al-tashih): The act of correcting or authenticating a legal opinion within the Hanafi school, indicating that it is the relied-upon position.
ظاهر الرواية (zahir al-riwaya): The transmitted narrations from Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani that are widely known and relied upon, forming the core of the Hanafi madhhab.
الترجيح (al-tarjih): The process of giving preference to one legal opinion over another when they conflict, based on specific criteria.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Because of changing times and necessity (darura).
C) Because it was more beneficial for the waqf.
D) Because it was based on istihsan.

Key Takeaway
When two opinions are equally corrected, the mufti must apply these ten rules—from authorial rank to changing custom—to determine the stronger view.

Tenth Rule of Tarjīḥ: Preferring Stronger Evidence
Rasm al-Mufti rule on tarjīḥ by evidence strength

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the tenth tarjīḥ rule to resolve conflicting corrections by identifying stronger evidence and other preponderant factors.

Core Idea
When tashīḥ conflict, prefer the opinion with stronger evidence—this is the tenth tarjīḥ rule.

In the Hanafī school, muftīs often face conflicting corrections (tashīḥ) for two equally authentic opinions. This passage presents the tenth rule for resolving such conflicts: prioritize the opinion whose evidence (dalīl) is clearer or stronger. It also lists other preponderant factors when no explicit correction is given.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conflict
Determine that two valid corrections (tashīḥ) exist for the same issue, each pointing to a different opinion.
The conflict is between two equally authentic positions within the school.
2
Assess evidence strength
Examine the evidence (dalīl) behind each opinion. The one with clearer or stronger evidence should be preferred.
This requires the muftī's own jihād al-ra'y if no explicit tashīḥ is given.
3
Apply the rule
Prefer the opinion with stronger evidence. If no explicit correction exists, apply other preponderant factors: being in the mutūn, the Imām's statement, or Ẓāhir al-Riwāyah.
These factors are hierarchical; Ẓāhir al-Riwāyah carries highest weight.
4
Confirm with other factors
If one opinion has multiple preponderant factors, it becomes the muftā bihī.
This rule is part of the comprehensive tarjīḥ methodology in Hanafī iftā'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العاشر : ما إذا كان أحدهما دليله أوضح وأظهر، كما تقدم أن الترجيح بقوة الدليل. فحيث وجد تصحيحان، ورأى من كان له أهلية النظر في الدليل أن دليل أحدهما أقوى، فالعمل به أولى. هذا كله إذا تعارض التصحيح؛ لأن كل واحد من القولين مساو للآخر في الصحة فإذا كان في أحدهما زيادة قوة من جهة أخرى، يكون العمل به أولى من العمل بالآخر. وكذا إذا لم يصرح بتصحيح واحد من القولين فيقدم ما فيه مرجح من هذه المرجحات، ككونه في المتون، أو قول الإمام، أو ظاهر الرواية. إلخ.
The tenth: When one of the two has a clearer and more apparent evidence, as previously mentioned that preference is by strength of evidence. So wherever two corrections are found, and someone who has the capacity to examine the evidence sees that the evidence of one is stronger, then acting upon it is more appropriate. All of this is when the corrections conflict; because each of the two statements is equal to the other in authenticity. So if in one there is additional strength from another aspect, acting upon it is more appropriate than acting upon the other. Likewise, if he did not explicitly correct one of the two statements, then that which has a preponderant factor from these preponderant factors is given precedence, such as it being in the texts (mutūn), or the statement of the Imam, or Ẓāhir al-Riwāyah, etc.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage states the tenth rule of tarjīḥ (preference) when there are conflicting tashīḥ (corrections) of two legal opinions. The ruling: when two opinions are equally authentic, but one has stronger evidence (dalīl), the muftī should prefer the one with stronger evidence. The author expands on this: if no explicit correction is given, the muftī should apply other preponderant factors—being in the matn (core text) of the madhhab, being the statement of Imām Abū Ḥanīfah, or being from Ẓāhir al-Riwāyah. This is a methodological rule for muftīs to follow when choosing between conflicting positions within the Hanafī school.

Key Terms
ترجيح (tarjīḥ): The act of giving preference to one legal opinion over another based on preponderant factors.
تصحيح (tashīḥ): Explicitly declaring a particular opinion as the correct or relied-upon position.
ظاهر الرواية (Ẓāhir al-Riwāyah): The six authoritative books of Imam Muḥammad ibn al-Ḥasan al-Shaybānī that form the foundation of the Hanafī school.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Mafhum in Custom and Riwayat: The Hanafi Qawida
Qawida on mafhum in non-Shar'i speech with furu' from custom and riwayat

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi qawida 'takhsis does not negate in Shari' speech but does in custom and riwayat' to distinguish between divine texts and ijtihadi statements when issuing fatwas.

Core Idea
Specification negates in custom, transactions, and mujtahid narrations, but not in the Qur'an and Sunnah.

A mufti often encounters statements from earlier mujtahids ('riwayat') and customary phrases ('mutafahim al-nas'). A critical error is to treat all such statements like divine texts. This lesson equips you with a foundational qawida that clarifies when specification implies negation of what is not mentioned. Mastering this rule ensures accurate derivation of rulings from non-Shar'i sources.

Guided Path
1
State the qawida and its two halves
Read the qawida: 'Specification by mention does not indicate negation in the Lawgiver's addresses, but does indicate in people's understanding, custom, transactions, and rational matters.' Identify the two domains.
The first half applies to Qur'an and Sunnah (including marfu' hadith). The second half applies to everything else.
2
Analyze the furuat from iqrar and zakat
Examine the example 'You have nothing more than one hundred dirhams' – acknowledgment of exactly one hundred. Also the riwaya about pasturing livestock implying no zakat on non-pasturing. Verify that these are consistent with the qawida.
In custom, specifying a number negates both excess and deficiency. In riwayat, specifying a condition negates its opposite.
3
Identify the istithna' (exception)
The source notes an exception: when a Companion's statement is marfu' (not based on ijtihad), it has the ruling of the Lawgiver's speech, so mafhum is not considered. Understand why this exception exists.
Mafhum is valid in hujjat al-sahaba when it is based on ra'y, not when it is marfu'.
4
Apply to a new scenario
Given a statement from a later Hanafi text: 'Wiping is valid on khuffs (leather socks) for one day and night for a resident.' Using the qawida, what does this imply for a traveler?
In riwayat, specification of 'resident' implies that the traveler has a different ruling (three days). This is valid because it is from a mujtahid's statement.
5
Connect to fatwa practice
As a mufti, when you see a ruling in a fiqh book, first ask: Is this from the Qur'an/Sunnah (direct words of Lawgiver) or from a mujtahid's ijtihad? Apply the appropriate mafhum rule.
This distinction is essential to avoid conflating primary texts with secondary ijtihad.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعتبار المفهوم في غير كلام الشارع]
قال في شرح التحرير بعد قوله غير معتبر في كلام الشارع فقط: فقد نقل الشيخ جلال الدين الخبازي في حاشية "الهداية" عن شمس الأئمة الكردري: "أن تخصيص الشيء بالذكر لا يدل على نفي الحكم عما عداه في خطابات الشارع فأما في متفاهم الناس وعرفهم، وفي المعاملات ،والعقليات يدل. وتداوله المتأخرون، وعليه ما في "خزانة الأكمل" و"الخانية". لو قال: "ما لك عليّ أكثر من مائة درهم كان إقرارا بالمائة، ولا يشكل عليه عدم لزوم شيء في "ما لك علي أكثر من مائة درهم ولا أقل"، كما لا يخفى على المتأمل.
ومنه أقوال "النهر": المفهوم معتبر في الروايات اتفاقا، وفي حج الصحابة، قال: وينبغي تقييده بما يدرك بالرأي لا ما لم يدرك به. أي لأن قول الصحابي إذا كان لا يدرك بالرأي – أي بالاجتهاد له حكم
سل الله
المرفوع، فيكون من كلام الشارع ، والمفهوم فيه غير معتبر. فالمراد
بالروايات ما روي في الكتب عن المجتهدين من الصحابة وغيرهم. وفي "النهر" أيضا عند سنن الوضوء: مفاهيم الكتب حجة بخلاف أكثر
11
مفاهيم النصوص. وفي غاية "البيان" عند قوله : وليس على المرأة أن تنقض ضفائرها، احترز بالمرأة عن الرجل. وتخصيص الشيء في الروايات يدل على نفي ما عداه ،بالاتفاق بخلاف النصوص فإن فيها لا يدل على نفي ما عداه عندنا.
Consideration of Mafhum in other than the Speech of the Lawgiver. He said in Sharh al-Tahrir after his statement 'not considered except in the speech of the Lawgiver': The Shaykh Jalal al-Din al-Khabbazi narrated in his gloss on al-Hidayah from Shams al-A'imma al-Kardari: 'That specification of something by mention does not indicate negation of the ruling from what is other than it in the addresses of the Lawgiver, but in the understanding of people and their custom, and in transactions, and rational matters, it does indicate. And it is taken up by the later scholars, and upon it is what is in Khizanat al-Akmal and al-Khaniyyah. If someone says, "You have nothing more than one hundred dirhams against me," it is an acknowledgment of one hundred, and it is not problematic regarding the non-obligation of anything in "You have nothing more than one hundred dirhams nor less," as is not hidden from the one who reflects.' And from it are the statements of al-Nahr: Mafhum is considered in the riwayat (narrations) by agreement, and in the hujja (proof) of the Companions. He said: It should be restricted to what is attainable by opinion, not what is not attainable. That is because the statement of a Companion, if it is not attainable by opinion – i.e., by ijtihad – has the ruling of a marfu‘ (raised) hadith, thus it becomes from the speech of the Lawgiver, and mafhum in it is not considered. Thus what is meant by riwayat is what is narrated in books from the mujtahids among the Companions and others. And in al-Nahr also, under The Sunnahs of Wudu: The implications (mafahim) of books are proof, contrary to most implications of the texts. And in Ghayat al-Bayan at his statement: 'And the woman is not required to undo her braids,' he restricted 'woman' as distinct from man. And specification of something in riwayat indicates negation of what is other than it by agreement, contrary to the texts, for in them it does not indicate negation of what is other than it according to us.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage clarifies a crucial rule for the mufti: the principle that specification (takhsis) does not negate what is not mentioned applies only to the speech of the Lawgiver. In human custom, transactions, and rational discourse, specification does imply negation. The example from al-Khaniyyah and Khizanat al-Akmal illustrates this: if someone says 'you have nothing more than one hundred dirhams' it is an acknowledgment of one hundred dirhams; adding 'nor less' does not create confusion because in common understanding, specifying a number negates both excess and deficiency. Similarly, in narrations from mujtahids (riwayat), mafhum is considered by agreement. However, when a Companion's statement is based on ijtihad and not on direct revelation (i.e., not marfu'), then mafhum is considered; if it is marfu' (like a hadith), it falls under the speech of the Lawgiver and mafhum is not considered. The passage also notes that in the books (kutub) — meaning the works of the mujtahids — their implications are hujja (proof), unlike many implications of the primary texts (nusus) as per the Hanafi position. The example from Ghayat al-Bayan — 'not required to undo braids' — shows that specifying 'woman' is meant to exclude men, implying that men might be required. In riwayat, such specification indicates negation of the ruling for others. Thus the mufti must distinguish between the Lawgiver's speech (where mafhum mukhalafa is invalid) and the speech of mujtahids and people's custom (where it is valid).

Key Terms
متفاهم الناس (mutafahim al-nas): The common understanding of people in their customary speech and transactions.
الروايات (al-riwayat): Narrations from mujtahid scholars (Companions and later) recorded in books, as opposed to nusus (primary revelatory texts).
حج الصحابة (hujjat al-sahaba): The statements of the Companions considered as evidence, but restricted to those based on opinion (ijtihad), not marfu'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In which of the following is mafhum mukhalafa considered valid according to the Hanafi school?
A) In the Qur'an and Sunnah
B) In people's custom and transactions
C) In marfu' hadith
D) In none of the above

Key Takeaway
Specification negates in custom, transactions, and riwayat, but not in the Lawgiver's speech.
